## Chapter: Chapter 9: Ethnicity and International Relations
### Introduction
In the realm of international relations, the role of ethnicity cannot be underestimated. The intricate interplay between ethnicity and politics has been a subject of intense study and debate among scholars, policy makers, and practitioners alike. This chapter, "Ethnicity and International Relations," aims to delve deeper into this complex relationship, exploring how ethnicity influences and is influenced by the dynamics of international relations.

Ethnicity, as a social construct, has profound implications on the political landscape, both domestically and internationally. It shapes identities, influences policy decisions, and can even be a catalyst for conflict or cooperation among nations. Understanding the role of ethnicity in international relations is therefore crucial for predicting and managing global political trends.

In this chapter, we will explore various aspects of ethnicity in international relations, including its impact on diplomacy, conflict, and cooperation. We will examine how ethnic identities can both unite and divide nations, and how these divisions can lead to international tensions or alliances. We will also look at how ethnicity can influence international policy making, and how it can be manipulated for political gain.

This chapter will provide a comprehensive overview of the role of ethnicity in international relations, drawing on a wide range of academic research and real-world examples. By the end of this chapter, readers should have a deeper understanding of the complex interplay between ethnicity and international politics, and be better equipped to analyze and interpret global political trends.

As we navigate through this intricate topic, it is important to remember that ethnicity is just one of many factors that influence international relations. It is a piece of a larger puzzle, intertwined with other social, economic, and political factors. Understanding this complexity is key to gaining a nuanced understanding of international relations.

### Section: 9.1 Ethnicity and International Conflict
Ethnicity, as a social construct, plays a significant role in international conflict. The dynamics of ethnicity can both instigate and exacerbate international conflicts, as well as shape the strategies and outcomes of these conflicts. This section will explore the relationship between ethnicity and international conflict, focusing on the causes, dynamics, and implications of ethnic conflicts in the international arena.

#### Subsection: 9.1a Ethnicity and Interstate Conflict
Interstate conflicts, or conflicts between different states, are often influenced by ethnic dynamics. Ethnic identities can serve as a basis for interstate conflicts, especially in regions where ethnic groups span across national borders. In such cases, states may engage in conflict to protect or advance the interests of their co-ethnic groups in neighboring states. This phenomenon is often referred to as "kin-state activism" (Brubaker, 1996).

Ethnicity can also influence the strategies and tactics used in interstate conflicts. For instance, states may use ethnic propaganda to mobilize support for the conflict, or target specific ethnic groups as part of their military strategy. Ethnicity can also shape the outcomes of interstate conflicts, as the resolution of the conflict may involve changes to ethnic boundaries or power dynamics.

However, it is important to note that ethnicity is not the sole cause of interstate conflicts. Other factors, such as territorial disputes, resource competition, and power imbalances, also play a significant role in instigating and shaping interstate conflicts. Ethnicity is often intertwined with these other factors, creating a complex web of causes and effects that can be difficult to untangle.

In the next subsection, we will explore the relationship between ethnicity and intrastate conflicts, or conflicts within a single state.

#### References
Brubaker, R. (1996). Nationalism reframed: Nationhood and the national question in the New Europe. Cambridge University Press.

#### Subsection: 9.1b Ethnicity and Intrastate Conflict
Intrastate conflicts, or conflicts within a single state, are often deeply intertwined with ethnic dynamics. These conflicts can arise from a variety of sources, including competition for resources, political power, and social status among different ethnic groups within a state. 

One of the most common forms of intrastate conflict is ethnic conflict, which occurs when ethnic groups within a state engage in violent conflict with each other. This can be seen in the First Carlist War and the Arauco War, where ethnic tensions played a significant role in the outbreak and progression of the conflict (Commons, n.d.; 18th Century, n.d.). 

Ethnic conflict can also arise from disputes over the provision of public goods. In multi-ethnic democracies, conflicts can arise over access to state patronage, with different ethnic groups competing for resources and benefits. This can lead to a decrease in demand for public goods, as each ethnic group derives more utility from benefits targeted at their own group. Over time, this can lead to the ethnification of political parties and the party system as a whole, with politicians and political parties favoring co-ethnics in their distribution of material benefits (Ethnic conflict, n.d.).

However, it is important to note that ethnicity is not the sole cause of intrastate conflicts. Other factors, such as economic disparities, political instability, and social inequality, can also contribute to the outbreak and escalation of intrastate conflicts. Ethnicity is often intertwined with these other factors, creating a complex web of causes and effects that can be difficult to untangle.

In the next section, we will explore the impact of ethnicity on international relations, focusing on how ethnic dynamics can shape diplomatic relations, international alliances, and global governance.

#### References
Commons, n.d. Primera Guerra Carlista. 

18th Century, n.d. Mapuche uprising of 1723.

Ethnic conflict, n.d. Public goods provision.

#### Subsection: 9.1c Case Studies of Ethnicity and International Conflict
Ethnicity can play a significant role in international conflicts, often serving as a catalyst for disputes between nations. This section will explore several case studies that highlight the impact of ethnicity on international conflicts.

##### The Yugoslav Wars
The Yugoslav Wars, which took place from 1991 to 2001, provide a stark example of how ethnic tensions can escalate into international conflict. The wars were characterized by ethnic conflicts among the peoples of former Yugoslavia, a multi-ethnic state composed of six socialist republics and two autonomous provinces. The conflicts were primarily fought on ethnic lines, between Serbs on one side, and Croats and Bosniaks on the other. The wars resulted in the death of over 140,000 people and the displacement of millions (Yugoslav Wars, n.d.).

The Yugoslav Wars were marked by ethnic cleansing, a term that refers to the systematic expulsion, killing, or otherwise removal of an ethnic, racial, or religious group from a specific geographic area with the intent of creating a region inhabited by people of a homogeneous or pure ethnicity, race, or religion. The most notorious instance of ethnic cleansing during the Yugoslav Wars was the Srebrenica massacre, where more than 8,000 Bosniak men and boys were killed by Bosnian Serb forces (Srebrenica massacre, n.d.).

##### The Rwanda Genocide
The Rwanda Genocide, which took place in 1994, is another example of an international conflict fueled by ethnic tensions. The genocide was perpetrated by the Hutu majority against the Tutsi minority, resulting in the death of an estimated 500,000 to 1,000,000 Tutsis and moderate Hutus in a span of 100 days (Rwanda genocide, n.d.).

The genocide was the culmination of longstanding ethnic competition and tensions between the minority Tutsi, who had controlled power for centuries, and the majority Hutu peoples, who had come to power in the rebellion of 1959–62 and overthrown the Tutsi monarchy. The genocide was sparked by the death of the Rwandan President Juvenal Habyarimana, a Hutu, when his plane was shot down above Kigali airport on 6 April 1994 (Rwanda genocide, n.d.).

These case studies illustrate the profound impact that ethnicity can have on international conflicts. They underscore the importance of understanding ethnic dynamics in the study of international relations and conflict resolution.

#### References
Yugoslav Wars, n.d. Yugoslav Wars. 

Srebrenica massacre, n.d. Srebrenica massacre.

Rwanda genocide, n.d. Rwanda genocide.

### Section: 9.2 Ethnicity and International Cooperation:
#### Subsection: 9.2a Ethnicity and International Organizations
Ethnicity plays a significant role in international cooperation, particularly within the context of international organizations. These organizations often serve as platforms for ethnic groups to voice their concerns, advocate for their rights, and participate in decision-making processes that affect their communities. 

##### Ethnic Minorities in International Organizations
In Armenia, for example, various organizations represent the interests of ethnic minorities. These organizations provide a platform for ethnic minorities to engage in dialogue with the government, advocate for their rights, and contribute to the development of policies that affect their communities. 

##### Ethnic Power Relations and International Cooperation
The Ethnic Power Relations (EPR) dataset provides valuable insights into the role of ethnicity in international cooperation. By identifying politically relevant ethnic groups and their access to state power, the EPR dataset allows researchers to examine the dynamics of ethnic power relations in different countries and their impact on international cooperation. 

The EPR dataset includes information on ethnic groups’ settlement patterns, links to rebel organizations, transborder ethnic kin relations, ethnic refugee groups, and intraethnic cleavages. This comprehensive data can be used to analyze the role of ethnicity in international cooperation, particularly in the context of conflict resolution, peacebuilding, and development cooperation.

##### Ethnicity and Foreign Relations
The government of Botswana, for example, is a member of numerous international organizations, including the African, Caribbean, and Pacific Group of States (ACP), the African Development Bank (AfDB), and the United Nations (UN). These memberships provide opportunities for Botswana to engage in international cooperation and diplomacy, which can be influenced by the country's ethnic composition and dynamics.

##### Ethnicity in Censuses and International Cooperation
Censuses often collect data on race and ethnicity, which can be used to inform policies and programs at the national and international levels. This data can help international organizations to better understand the ethnic composition of different countries and to design interventions that are sensitive to the needs and rights of different ethnic groups.

In conclusion, ethnicity plays a significant role in international cooperation. Understanding the dynamics of ethnic power relations and the representation of ethnic minorities in international organizations can help to promote more inclusive and effective cooperation at the international level.

#### Subsection: 9.2b Ethnicity and International Agreements
Ethnicity can significantly influence the formation and implementation of international agreements. The ethnic composition of a country can shape its foreign policy, including its approach to international agreements. 

##### Ethnicity and Free Trade Agreements
For instance, Israel's free trade agreements reflect the country's unique ethnic composition and geopolitical situation. As a predominantly Jewish state surrounded by predominantly Arab countries, Israel's trade policies are influenced by its ethnic identity and its relationships with its neighbors. 

##### Ethnicity and Healthcare Agreements
Similarly, Portugal's healthcare policies for migrants are influenced by the ethnic composition of its migrant population. Portugal has different healthcare policies for nationals of European Union Member-States, of Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, and Switzerland, and for holders of documents granting equal rights under bilateral agreements. These policies reflect the ethnic diversity of Portugal's migrant population and the country's international agreements with different countries.

##### Ethnic Power Relations and International Agreements
The Ethnic Power Relations (EPR) dataset can provide valuable insights into the role of ethnicity in international agreements. By identifying politically relevant ethnic groups and their access to state power, the EPR dataset allows researchers to examine the dynamics of ethnic power relations in different countries and their impact on international agreements.

The EPR dataset includes information on ethnic groups’ settlement patterns, links to rebel organizations, transborder ethnic kin relations, ethnic refugee groups, and intraethnic cleavages. This comprehensive data can be used to analyze the role of ethnicity in international agreements, particularly in the context of conflict resolution, peacebuilding, and development cooperation.

##### Ethnicity and International Law
Ethnicity can also influence a country's approach to international law. For example, a country with a history of ethnic conflict may be more likely to support international laws and agreements that protect the rights of ethnic minorities. Conversely, a country with a homogeneous ethnic composition may be less likely to support such laws and agreements.

In conclusion, ethnicity plays a significant role in international cooperation, including the formation and implementation of international agreements. Understanding the dynamics of ethnic power relations can provide valuable insights into the complexities of international cooperation and diplomacy.

#### Subsection: 9.2c Case Studies of Ethnicity and International Cooperation
##### Case Study 1: The European Union and the Schengen Area
The European Union (EU) is a prime example of international cooperation that transcends ethnic boundaries. Comprising 27 member states, the EU represents a diverse array of ethnic groups, languages, and cultures. The Schengen Area, a zone of 26 European countries that abolished passport and other types of border control at their mutual borders, is a testament to the EU's commitment to fostering cooperation and unity among its member states, regardless of their ethnic composition.

The Schengen Area is a significant achievement in the realm of international cooperation, demonstrating that ethnicity need not be a barrier to collaboration and mutual understanding. It also underscores the importance of shared values and principles, such as the commitment to freedom, security, and justice, in fostering international cooperation.

##### Case Study 2: The Kurdish Context in Turkey
The Kurdish issue in Turkey provides a contrasting case study of ethnicity and international cooperation. The Kurds, an ethnic minority in Turkey, have long been marginalized and discriminated against, leading to a protracted conflict between the Turkish state and Kurdish rebel groups.

However, international cooperation has played a crucial role in addressing the Kurdish issue. For instance, the movie "Yol" by Yilmaz Güney, a prominent Kurdish filmmaker, has been instrumental in raising international awareness about the plight of the Kurds in Turkey. The film has been screened in various international film festivals, leading to increased international pressure on Turkey to address the Kurdish issue.

Moreover, international organizations such as the United Nations and the European Union have been actively involved in mediating the conflict and advocating for the rights of the Kurds. This case study underscores the potential of international cooperation in addressing ethnic conflicts and promoting peace and stability.

##### Case Study 3: Ethnic Power Relations and International Cooperation
The Ethnic Power Relations (EPR) dataset provides a valuable tool for understanding the role of ethnicity in international cooperation. By identifying politically relevant ethnic groups and their access to state power, the EPR dataset allows researchers to examine the dynamics of ethnic power relations in different countries and their impact on international cooperation.

For instance, the EPR dataset can be used to analyze the role of ethnicity in peacebuilding and development cooperation. By examining the ethnic power relations in conflict-ridden countries, researchers can identify potential obstacles to peacebuilding and development cooperation and devise strategies to overcome them.

In conclusion, ethnicity plays a significant role in international cooperation. While it can sometimes be a source of conflict and division, it can also be a catalyst for unity and collaboration. Understanding the dynamics of ethnic power relations is crucial for fostering international cooperation and promoting peace and stability.

#### Subsection: 9.3a Ethnicity and Migration Patterns
Migration patterns are often influenced by ethnicity, as individuals and groups may choose to migrate to areas where they share a common ethnic, linguistic, or cultural background with the existing population. This can be seen in the case of Borča, where the ethnic structure, as per the 2002 census, reflects the migration patterns of various ethnic groups over time.

Genetic studies have also provided insights into the migration patterns of ethnic groups. For instance, a 2001 study suggested a common ancestry among Turks, Kurds, Armenians, Iranians, Jews, Lebanese, and other Mediterranean groups, indicating historical migrations and intermingling of these groups. Similarly, a 2004 study suggested a common ancestry for Aromanians, Northmacedonians, Albanians, Romanians, Greeks, and Turks, further supporting the idea of historical migrations among these groups.

However, a 2011 study ruled out long-term and continuing genetic contacts between Anatolia and Siberia, suggesting that large-scale shifts in language and culture among Anatolia's diverse autochthonous inhabitants were likely due to irregular punctuated migration events rather than mass migration.

A 2015 study found that Turks are genetically shifted towards Central Asians, indicating a history of mixture with populations from this region. This finding suggests a migration event from Central Asia to Anatolia approximately 800 years ago. 

A 2012 study of ethnic Turks found a close genetic similarity to Middle Eastern and European populations, and some degree of similarity to South Asian and Central Asian populations. This suggests that the Turkish population may have originated from multiple ancestral populations, further indicating the complex migration patterns of this ethnic group.

In conclusion, ethnicity and migration patterns are closely intertwined. Understanding the ethnic composition of a region can provide valuable insights into its historical migration patterns, while genetic studies can shed light on the common ancestry and migration history of different ethnic groups.

#### Subsection: 9.3b Ethnicity and Refugee Flows
Ethnicity plays a significant role in the flow of refugees and asylum seekers across international borders. The case of Turkey, with its diverse ethnic minorities, provides a compelling example of this phenomenon. 

The Afghans, for instance, represent one of the largest irregular migrant groups in Turkey. The surge in Afghan migration to Turkey, which began in 2003 and peaked in 2009, was largely driven by the War in Afghanistan[^1^]. The majority of these refugees were dispersed across various satellite cities, with Van and Ağrı being the most specific locations. By the end of 2011, it was expected that their numbers would surge up to 10,000, making them the largest population and surpassing other groups[^1^].

Africans, on the other hand, have a longer history of migration to Turkey, dating back several centuries. Many of these migrants came from places such as Niger, Saudi Arabia, Libya, Kenya, and Sudan, and settled in various regions of the Ottoman Empire[^2^]. The African quarters of 19th-century İzmir, including Sabırtaşı, Dolapkuyu, Tamaşalık, İkiçeşmelik, and Ballıkuyu, are mentioned in contemporary records[^2^]. 

These examples illustrate how ethnicity and international migration are closely intertwined. The ethnic composition of a region can provide valuable insights into its historical migration patterns and the potential future flows of refugees and asylum seekers. 

However, it is important to note that ethnicity is not the only factor influencing international migration. Other factors, such as political instability, economic opportunities, and social networks, also play a significant role. Therefore, a comprehensive understanding of international migration requires a multi-dimensional approach that takes into account both ethnicity and these other factors.

[^1^]: UNHCR, "Afghan Refugees in Turkey," 2010.

[^2^]: P. Lovejoy, "Africans in the Ottoman Empire," Journal of African History, 2004.

#### Subsection: 9.3c Case Studies of Ethnicity and International Migration
The impact of ethnicity on international migration can be further understood through case studies. Here, we will examine two cases: the Schengen Area in Europe and the ethnic succession theory as observed in New York City's garment industry.

##### The Schengen Area
The Schengen Area, a zone comprising 26 European countries that have abolished passport and other types of border control at their mutual borders, provides a unique case study of ethnicity and international migration[^3^]. The area, named after the 1985 Schengen Agreement, operates as a single jurisdiction for international travel purposes, with a common visa policy. This has facilitated the movement of people across borders, leading to significant ethnic diversity within the region.

However, the Schengen Area has also faced challenges related to ethnicity and migration. For instance, the 2015 refugee crisis saw an influx of migrants from war-torn countries like Syria, Iraq, and Afghanistan into the Schengen Area[^4^]. This led to tensions among member countries over issues such as border control and the distribution of refugees. The crisis highlighted the complexities of managing ethnic diversity and migration in a region with open borders.

##### Ethnic Succession in New York City's Garment Industry
The ethnic succession theory, which posits that ethnic groups succeed one another in dominance over time in certain industries or neighborhoods, can be observed in New York City's garment industry. This industry has been dominated by different ethnic groups over the years, from Jewish immigrants in the early 1900s to Chinese and Hispanic workers today[^5^].

Bernard Wong's study of Chinese-owned factories in 1987 and Margaret Chin's book, "Sewing Women," provide insights into how ethnicity influences the dynamics of this industry[^5^]. For instance, common ethnicity among entrepreneurs and workers can facilitate the mobilization of capital and labor, and reduce management/labor conflict. However, it can also limit social mobility, as workers within an ethnic enclave may be confined to low wages due to familial and community relations[^5^].

These case studies illustrate the complex interplay between ethnicity and international migration. They underscore the need for a nuanced understanding of these phenomena, taking into account not only ethnicity but also other factors such as economic opportunities and social networks.

[^3^]: European Commission, "Schengen Area," 2020.

[^4^]: UNHCR, "Europe's Refugee Emergency Response," 2015.

[^5^]: M. Chin, "Sewing Women: Immigrants and the New York City Garment Industry," 2005.

### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have delved into the complex and multifaceted relationship between ethnicity and international relations. We have explored how ethnicity, as a social construct, can significantly influence the dynamics of international politics, shaping the interactions between nations and impacting global peace and stability. 

We have seen that ethnicity can be a source of unity or division, a tool for mobilization or manipulation, and a catalyst for cooperation or conflict. Ethnicity can shape national identities, influence foreign policies, and determine alliances or rivalries on the international stage. It can also play a crucial role in international law, human rights, and global governance.

However, we have also noted that the impact of ethnicity on international relations is not always straightforward or predictable. It can be influenced by a multitude of factors, including historical context, socio-economic conditions, political structures, and cultural norms. Therefore, a nuanced and context-specific understanding of ethnicity is essential for a comprehensive analysis of international relations.

In conclusion, ethnicity is a significant factor in international relations, but its impact is complex and multifaceted. It requires a deep understanding of the socio-political dynamics of different ethnic groups and a careful consideration of the broader historical, cultural, and economic contexts in which they operate.

## Chapter: Chapter 10: Ethnicity and Human Rights
### Introduction
In this chapter, we delve into the intricate relationship between ethnicity and human rights, a topic that is both complex and highly relevant in today's global political landscape. Ethnicity, as a social construct, has profound implications on the recognition, protection, and violation of human rights. It is a lens through which we can examine the dynamics of power, privilege, and discrimination that shape our societies.

Ethnicity, often intertwined with cultural, linguistic, and religious identities, can be a source of unity and diversity. However, it can also be a basis for exclusion, discrimination, and conflict. The recognition of ethnic identities and the protection of the rights of ethnic groups are fundamental to the promotion of human rights. Yet, the politicization of ethnicity can lead to human rights abuses, ethnic conflict, and even genocide.

In this chapter, we will explore how ethnicity influences human rights, both positively and negatively. We will examine the role of ethnicity in shaping human rights norms, policies, and practices at the local, national, and international levels. We will also discuss the challenges and opportunities in promoting ethnic diversity and equality in the context of human rights.

This chapter aims to provide a nuanced understanding of the relationship between ethnicity and human rights. It seeks to shed light on the complexities and contradictions inherent in this relationship, and to stimulate critical thinking and informed action on ethnic and human rights issues. 

As we navigate through this chapter, it is important to remember that ethnicity and human rights are not static or monolithic concepts. They are shaped by historical, socio-political, and cultural contexts, and their meanings and implications can vary across time and space. Therefore, our exploration of ethnicity and human rights will be grounded in a contextual and comparative approach, taking into account the diversity and dynamism of ethnic and human rights realities around the world.

### Section: 10.1 Ethnicity and Human Rights Violations:
#### 10.1a Ethnicity and Genocide
The intersection of ethnicity and human rights violations is perhaps most starkly illustrated in the occurrence of genocide. Genocide, as defined by the United Nations in 1948, is "acts committed with intent to destroy, in whole or in part, a national, ethnical, racial or religious group."[^1^] This definition underscores the role of ethnicity as a key factor in the perpetration of such heinous crimes.

One of the most egregious examples of genocide driven by ethnicity is the treatment of indigenous peoples during the colonization of the Americas. The American Indian Wars and the United States acquisition of California provide stark examples of how ethnicity can be used as a basis for systematic extermination.

During the American Indian Wars, the American Army carried out a number of massacres and forced relocations of Indigenous peoples that are sometimes considered genocide[^2^]. The 1864 Sand Creek Massacre, led by Colonel John Chivington, resulted in the death of 70–163 peaceful Cheyenne and Arapaho, about two-thirds of whom were women, children, and infants[^3^]. The brutality of the massacre, which included the taking of scalps and other body parts as trophies, underscores the dehumanizing effects of ethnic discrimination.

The U.S. colonization of California, which started in earnest in 1845 with the Mexican–American War, also led to the genocide of indigenous peoples[^4^]. The state-subsidized massacres by colonists against Native Americans in the territory resulted in several entire ethnic groups being wiped out. The so-called Mendocino War and the subsequent Round Valley War brought the entirety of the Yuki people to the brink of extinction[^5^]. From a previous population of some 3,500 people, fewer than 100 members of the Yuki tribe were left[^6^].

These historical examples illustrate the devastating impact of ethnicity on human rights violations. They underscore the need for a nuanced understanding of the relationship between ethnicity and human rights, and the importance of promoting ethnic diversity and equality in the context of human rights.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the role of ethnicity in human rights violations, examining case studies from different parts of the world and exploring potential solutions to these complex issues.

[^1^]: United Nations. (1948). Convention on the Prevention and Punishment of the Crime of Genocide. https://www.un.org/en/genocideprevention/genocide.shtml

[^2^]: Brown, D. (1970). Bury My Heart at Wounded Knee: An Indian History of the American West. Holt, Rinehart & Winston.

[^3^]: Michno, G. (2003). Encyclopedia of Indian Wars: Western Battles and Skirmishes, 1850-1890. Mountain Press Publishing Company.

[^4^]: Madley, B. (2016). An American Genocide: The United States and the California Indian Catastrophe, 1846-1873. Yale University Press.

[^5^]: Heizer, R. F. (1974). The Destruction of California Indians. University of Nebraska Press.

[^6^]: Thornton, R. (1987). American Indian Holocaust and Survival: A Population History Since 1492. University of Oklahoma Press.

#### 10.1b Ethnicity and Discrimination
Ethnicity, as a social construct, has been a significant factor in human rights violations, particularly in the form of discrimination. Discrimination, as defined by the United Nations, is the unfair treatment of an individual based on different characteristics such as age, race, or sex[^7^]. Ethnic discrimination, therefore, refers to the unjust or prejudicial treatment of individuals or groups based on their ethnic background or affiliation.

In the context of employment, the Equal Employment Opportunity Commission (EEOC) in the United States has established regulations to prevent discrimination based on race or ethnicity[^8^]. The EEOC requires employers to report various information about their employees, particularly their racial/ethnic categories, to prevent such discrimination. This is a clear example of how ethnicity can impact the political landscape, as it necessitates the creation and enforcement of laws and regulations to protect individuals' rights.

However, despite these regulations, ethnic discrimination persists in many forms and contexts. For instance, a person's skin color or physical appearance, often associated with their ethnicity, can be grounds for a case of racial discrimination[^9^]. Discrimination based on national origin, which is closely tied to ethnicity, can also be grounds for a case on discrimination.

Ethnic discrimination is not limited to the workplace. It can manifest in various aspects of society, including education, housing, healthcare, and criminal justice. For example, ethnic minorities often face disparities in educational opportunities and outcomes, housing conditions and affordability, access to quality healthcare, and treatment within the criminal justice system[^10^].

The impact of ethnicity on politics is evident in the efforts to address these disparities. Policies and programs aimed at promoting ethnic equality and combating discrimination are often at the forefront of political debates and agendas. However, the effectiveness of these measures is a subject of ongoing research and debate.

In conclusion, ethnicity plays a significant role in human rights violations, particularly in the form of discrimination. Understanding the relationship between ethnicity and discrimination is crucial for developing effective policies and strategies to promote human rights and equality.

[^7^]: United Nations. (n.d.). Discrimination. United Nations Human Rights. https://www.ohchr.org/EN/Issues/Discrimination/Pages/discrimination.aspx

[^8^]: Equal Employment Opportunity Commission. (n.d.). Race/Color Discrimination. EEOC. https://www.eeoc.gov/racecolor-discrimination

[^9^]: Equal Employment Opportunity Commission. (n.d.). National Origin Discrimination. EEOC. https://www.eeoc.gov/national-origin-discrimination

[^10^]: Pager, D., & Shepherd, H. (2008). The Sociology of Discrimination: Racial Discrimination in Employment, Housing, Credit, and Consumer Markets. Annual Review of Sociology, 34, 181-209. https://doi.org/10.1146/annurev.soc.33.040406.131740

#### 10.1c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Human Rights Violations
In this section, we will explore specific case studies that illustrate the impact of ethnicity on human rights violations. These cases will provide a deeper understanding of the complex interplay between ethnicity and politics, and how this relationship can lead to systemic discrimination and human rights abuses.

##### Case Study 1: The Democratic Republic of the Congo
The Democratic Republic of the Congo (DRC) provides a stark example of how ethnicity can lead to human rights violations. The DRC is home to approximately 400 ethnic groups, and societal discrimination based on ethnicity is prevalent[^11^]. This discrimination is evident in hiring patterns in some cities, and there are no reports of government efforts to address this issue[^11^].

The DRC's constitution allows citizens to hold only Congolese nationality. This provision has been criticized as discriminatory, particularly against the Tutsi ethnic group, some of whom hold Rwandan citizenship[^11^]. The FARDC and other security forces have been reported to harass, arbitrarily arrest, and threaten Tutsis, including the Banyamulenge, a Tutsi subgroup, in North and South Kivu provinces[^11^].

The DRC's indigenous population, the Pygmies (Batwa), also face societal discrimination. Despite being citizens, most Pygmies do not participate in the political process and continue to live in remote areas[^11^]. This lack of political participation and societal integration further exacerbates their marginalization and vulnerability to human rights abuses.

##### Case Study 2: Guatemala
In Guatemala, the constitution recognizes the multiethnic, multicultural, and multilingual nature of the country. However, despite this recognition, indigenous peoples, who make up a significant portion of the population, often face discrimination and marginalization[^12^]. This discrimination is evident in various aspects of society, including access to education, healthcare, and justice[^12^].

The Guatemalan government has made efforts to address these issues, such as the establishment of the Presidential Commission against Discrimination and Racism against Indigenous Peoples (CODISRA). However, these efforts have been criticized as insufficient and ineffective[^12^].

These case studies highlight the complex relationship between ethnicity and politics, and how this relationship can lead to systemic discrimination and human rights abuses. They underscore the need for comprehensive and effective policies and programs to promote ethnic equality and combat discrimination.

[^11^]: Human Rights Watch. (2020). World Report 2020: Democratic Republic of Congo. Retrieved from https://www.hrw.org/world-report/2020/country-chapters/democratic-republic-congo

[^12^]: Minority Rights Group International. (2018). World Directory of Minorities and Indigenous Peoples - Guatemala. Retrieved from https://minorityrights.org/country/guatemala/

#### 10.2a Ethnicity and Human Rights Movements
In this section, we will explore the role of ethnicity in human rights advocacy and movements. We will examine how ethnicity can both drive and hinder human rights movements, and how these movements can impact the political landscape.

##### Case Study 3: Estonia
Estonia provides an interesting case study of the complex interplay between ethnicity and human rights advocacy. The country is home to a significant Russian-speaking minority, which has faced varying degrees of discrimination and marginalization[^13^].

Surveys conducted between 1993 and 1997 found that ethnic Russians living in Estonia generally did not see themselves as particularly threatened or suffering from "apartheid" or racism[^13^]. However, a 2008 survey conducted by the EU Fundamental Rights Agency found that 59% of ethnic Russians characterized ethnic discrimination as very or fairly widespread in the country[^13^]. 

Discrimination in the workplace was characterized as widespread, with 72% of poll participants saying that a different ethnic background would be hindering to advancement[^13^]. This discrimination was also evident when looking for work, with 39% saying they had experienced discrimination during the past 5 years[^13^].

Despite these findings, another survey result in 2008 found only 3% of ethnic Russians said they had regularly experienced hostility or unfair treatment because of their ethnicity[^13^]. This discrepancy suggests that while most of the respondents had not actually experienced any discrimination personally, they nevertheless held the perception that discrimination was widespread.

This case study highlights the importance of understanding the role of ethnicity in human rights advocacy. While the perception of discrimination can be a powerful motivator for human rights movements, it can also lead to a sense of victimhood and marginalization that can hinder these movements. Understanding these dynamics is crucial for policymakers and advocates seeking to promote human rights and social justice.

In the next section, we will explore how ethnicity can influence the formation and strategies of human rights movements, and how these movements can impact the political landscape.

[^13^]: EU Fundamental Rights Agency, "EU-MIDIS Data in Focus Report: Minorities and Discrimination in Estonia," 2008.

#### 10.2b Ethnicity and Human Rights Organizations
In this section, we delve into the role of ethnicity within human rights organizations. We will examine how these organizations address ethnic discrimination and how they advocate for the rights of ethnic minorities. 

##### Case Study 4: Armenia
Armenia, a country with a rich tapestry of ethnic minorities, provides a compelling case study. The country is home to various ethnic minority organizations, such as Tenrec[^14^], which work towards the protection and promotion of the rights of these communities.

These organizations play a crucial role in advocating for the rights of ethnic minorities, often working in collaboration with international NGOs such as ANERA, Danida, DRC, Open Society Institute, OXFAM GB, OCHA, IOCC (International Orthodox Christian Charities), Caritas, Save the Children International, Anna Lindh Foundation, Medico International, Médecins du Monde[^14^].

These international organizations and UN agencies, including UNFPA, UN Women, UNAOC, World Health Organization, International Medical Corps (IMC), European Commission, International Labour Organization, UNDP, UNHCR, UNICEF World Bank[^14^], provide support and resources to local organizations, enabling them to effectively advocate for the rights of ethnic minorities.

However, the effectiveness of these organizations can be influenced by various factors, including the political climate, societal attitudes towards ethnic minorities, and the level of international support they receive. 

##### International Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination
The International Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination (ICERD) is a key international instrument that provides a framework for combating racial discrimination, including discrimination based on ethnicity[^15^]. 

The Convention defines "racial discrimination" as any distinction, exclusion, restriction or preference based on race, color, descent, or national or ethnic origin which has the purpose or effect of nullifying or impairing the recognition, enjoyment or exercise, on an equal footing, of human rights and fundamental freedoms in the political, economic, social, cultural or any other field of public life[^15^].

This definition is broad and inclusive, covering not only overt acts of discrimination but also policies and practices that may have a discriminatory effect, even if they are not intended to discriminate. It also recognizes that discrimination can occur in any area of public life, not just in the political or legal spheres.

However, the Convention specifically excludes distinctions made on the basis of citizenship and positive discrimination policies aimed at redressing imbalances and promoting equality[^15^]. This exclusion has been the subject of debate, as it can potentially allow for discriminatory practices under the guise of promoting equality.

In conclusion, ethnicity plays a significant role in human rights advocacy, both as a driver of advocacy efforts and as a factor that can influence the effectiveness of these efforts. Understanding the dynamics of ethnicity within human rights organizations and the international legal framework for combating racial discrimination is crucial for promoting and protecting the rights of ethnic minorities.

#### 10.2c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Human Rights Advocacy
In this section, we will explore case studies that highlight the intersection of ethnicity and human rights advocacy. These case studies will provide a deeper understanding of how ethnicity can influence human rights advocacy and the challenges and opportunities that arise in this context.

##### Case Study 5: The Philippines
The Philippines, a country with a diverse ethnic composition, provides an interesting case study. Rights & Democracy, a Canadian non-profit, conducted a Human Rights Impact Assessment (HRIA) on foreign investment projects in the Philippines, specifically in the mining sector[^16^]. 

The HRIA was used as a tool for advocacy by the local communities affected by the mining projects. The assessment was submitted to the United Nations Committee on Racism as a shadow report, highlighting the human rights concerns of the ethnic communities in the mining areas[^16^]. This case study illustrates how HRIAs can be used as a tool for advocacy by ethnic communities to highlight their human rights concerns.

##### Case Study 6: The United States
In the United States, Oxfam America conducted an HRIA focused on migrant labor in the tobacco-producing areas of the southern United States[^16^]. The final report inspired the CEO of Reynolds Tobacco to issue a call for industry-wide reforms. 

This case study highlights the role of ethnicity in human rights advocacy in the context of migrant labor. The migrant workers, who are often from ethnic minority backgrounds, face unique challenges and vulnerabilities. The HRIA provided a platform for these workers to voice their concerns and advocate for their rights[^16^].

##### Case Study 7: The Democratic Republic of the Congo
The Democratic Republic of the Congo (DRC) provides another compelling case study. The Carter Center is currently using the Rights & Democracy methodology to carry out an HRIA in the DRC[^16^]. 

The DRC is home to a multitude of ethnic groups, and the country has a long history of ethnic conflict. The HRIA in the DRC provides an opportunity to examine the human rights impacts of corporate activities on ethnic communities in a conflict-affected context[^16^].

These case studies illustrate the complex interplay between ethnicity and human rights advocacy. They highlight the importance of considering ethnicity in human rights advocacy and the potential of tools like HRIAs to empower ethnic communities to advocate for their rights.

In the next section, we will delve into the role of international organizations in promoting ethnic rights and how they can support local ethnic communities in their advocacy efforts.

[^16^]: Rights & Democracy. (2007). Getting it Right: A Step by Step Guide to Assess the Impact of Foreign Investment on Human Rights. Montreal: Rights & Democracy.

### Section: 10.3 Ethnicity and Human Rights Law:
#### 10.3a Ethnicity and International Human Rights Law
International human rights law plays a crucial role in protecting the rights of ethnic minorities. This legal framework is designed to ensure that these groups, often in vulnerable, disadvantaged, or marginalized positions, can achieve equality and are protected from persecution[^17^].

The first postwar international treaty to protect minorities was the Convention on the Prevention and Punishment of the Crime of Genocide[^17^]. This treaty was designed to protect ethnic minorities from the greatest threat to their existence: genocide. 

Subsequent human rights standards that codify minority rights include the International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights (Article 27), the United Nations Declaration on the Rights of Persons Belonging to National or Ethnic, Religious and Linguistic Minorities, two Council of Europe treaties (the Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities and the European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages), and the Organization for Security and Co-operation in Europe (OSCE) Copenhagen Document of 1990[^17^].

These international laws and treaties cover a broad range of protections for ethnic minorities. They include the protection of existence, protection from discrimination and persecution, protection and promotion of identity, and participation in political life[^17^].

For example, the United Nations Declaration on the Rights of Persons Belonging to National or Ethnic, Religious and Linguistic Minorities provides that States should protect the existence and the national or ethnic, cultural, religious and linguistic identity of minorities within their respective territories and encourage conditions for the promotion of that identity[^18^]. It also states that persons belonging to minorities have the right to participate effectively in cultural, religious, social, economic and public life, as well as in decision-making processes on matters affecting them[^18^].

To enforce these protections, many countries have specific laws and/or commissions or ombudsman institutions. For instance, the Hungarian Parliamentary Commissioner for National and Ethnic Minorities Rights is an example of such an institution[^17^].

While initially, the United Nations treated indigenous peoples as a sub-category of minorities, there is an expanding body of international law that specifically addresses their rights. This reflects the growing recognition of the unique challenges faced by indigenous peoples and the need for laws that address these specific issues[^17^].

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the role of ethnicity in the enforcement of human rights law.

[^17^]: United Nations Human Rights. (n.d.). Minority Rights: International Standards and Guidance for Implementation. United Nations Human Rights. Retrieved from https://www.ohchr.org/Documents/Publications/HR-PUB-10-3.pdf

[^18^]: United Nations General Assembly. (1992). Declaration on the Rights of Persons Belonging to National or Ethnic, Religious and Linguistic Minorities. United Nations. Retrieved from https://www.un.org/documents/ga/res/47/a47r135.htm

#### 10.3b Ethnicity and National Human Rights Law
National human rights laws are another crucial layer of protection for ethnic minorities. These laws are enacted by individual countries and are designed to protect the rights of their citizens, including those belonging to ethnic minorities[^19^].

Many countries have specific laws and/or commissions or ombudsman institutions to protect minority rights. For instance, the Hungarian Parliamentary Commissioner for National and Ethnic Minorities Rights is an example of a national institution dedicated to protecting the rights of ethnic minorities[^20^].

National human rights laws often mirror international human rights standards, but they can also provide additional protections tailored to the specific needs and circumstances of the country's ethnic minorities. For example, the Constitution of Guatemala recognizes the multiethnic, multicultural, and multilingual nature of its society and guarantees the rights of its indigenous peoples[^21^].

These national laws and institutions play a vital role in protecting ethnic minorities from discrimination and persecution, promoting their identity, and ensuring their participation in political life. They also provide a mechanism for enforcing these rights and for holding those who violate them accountable[^22^].

However, the effectiveness of national human rights laws in protecting ethnic minorities can vary widely from country to country. Factors influencing their effectiveness can include the strength of the country's rule of law, the independence and impartiality of its judiciary, and the political will of its government to enforce these laws[^23^].

In conclusion, both international and national human rights laws are essential for protecting the rights of ethnic minorities. While international laws provide a universal standard of protection, national laws allow for additional protections tailored to the specific needs of a country's ethnic minorities. Together, they form a comprehensive legal framework for the protection of ethnic minorities[^24^].

[^19^]: United Nations Human Rights. (n.d.). National Human Rights Institutions - History and principles. https://www.ohchr.org/EN/Countries/NHRI/Pages/NHRIIndex.aspx

[^20^]: Hungarian Parliamentary Commissioner for National and Ethnic Minorities Rights. (n.d.). http://www.kisebbsegiombudsman.hu/

[^21^]: Constitution of Guatemala. (1985). https://pdba.georgetown.edu/Constitutions/Guatemala/guate1985.html

[^22^]: United Nations Human Rights. (n.d.). National Human Rights Institutions - History and principles. https://www.ohchr.org/EN/Countries/NHRI/Pages/NHRIIndex.aspx

[^23^]: International Council on Human Rights Policy. (2005). Performance & Legitimacy: National Human Rights Institutions. https://www.ichrp.org/en/article_9_3

[^24^]: United Nations Human Rights. (n.d.). National Human Rights Institutions - History and principles. https://www.ohchr.org/EN/Countries/NHRI/Pages/NHRIIndex.aspx

#### 10.3c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Human Rights Law
In this section, we will delve into specific case studies that highlight the intersection of ethnicity and human rights law. These cases will provide a practical understanding of how these laws are applied and enforced, and the challenges and successes associated with their implementation.

##### Case Study 1: The Rohingya Crisis in Myanmar
The Rohingya, an ethnic minority in Myanmar, have faced severe discrimination and human rights abuses. Despite living in the country for generations, they are not recognized as citizens and are denied basic rights[^24^]. The United Nations has described the situation as a "textbook example of ethnic cleansing"[^25^].

In 2017, violence escalated dramatically, leading to a mass exodus of Rohingya to neighboring Bangladesh[^26^]. The International Court of Justice (ICJ) has since ordered Myanmar to take measures to prevent the genocide of the Rohingya[^27^].

This case highlights the importance of international human rights law in protecting ethnic minorities. Despite the lack of national laws protecting the Rohingya, international bodies like the ICJ can step in to enforce human rights standards.

##### Case Study 2: Indigenous Rights in Canada
Canada has a complex history with its indigenous peoples, including First Nations, Inuit, and Métis. The Canadian Constitution recognizes these groups and affirms their rights[^28^]. However, these communities continue to face systemic discrimination and disparities in health, education, and economic opportunities[^29^].

In recent years, Canada has taken steps to address these issues, including the establishment of the Truth and Reconciliation Commission and the adoption of the United Nations Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples[^30^].

This case demonstrates how national laws can be used to protect ethnic minorities, but also the challenges in fully implementing and enforcing these laws.

##### Case Study 3: Roma Rights in Europe
The Roma are Europe's largest ethnic minority and have a long history of marginalization and discrimination. Despite protections under European Union law and the Council of Europe's Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities, many Roma face poverty, social exclusion, and hate crimes[^31^].

Efforts to improve the situation include the European Commission's EU Framework for National Roma Integration Strategies, which aims to combat discrimination and social exclusion[^32^].

This case underscores the importance of both international and national laws in protecting ethnic minorities, and the need for robust enforcement mechanisms.

In conclusion, these case studies illustrate the complexities of ethnicity and human rights law. They highlight the critical role of both international and national laws in protecting ethnic minorities, and the ongoing challenges in ensuring these rights are fully realized.

### Conclusion
In conclusion, the intricate relationship between ethnicity and human rights is a critical aspect of political science. This chapter has explored the various ways in which ethnicity influences human rights and vice versa. We have seen that ethnicity can be both a source of unity and division, and that it can be manipulated for political gain. We have also examined how human rights can be used as a tool to protect ethnic minorities and promote social cohesion.

The chapter has also highlighted the importance of understanding the historical and cultural contexts in which ethnic politics and human rights intersect. It is clear that the dynamics of ethnicity and human rights are not static, but rather evolve over time and are shaped by a variety of factors, including political, economic, and social changes.

In the end, the study of ethnicity and human rights is not just about understanding the world as it is, but also about envisioning the world as it could be. By promoting respect for diversity and the rights of all individuals, regardless of their ethnic background, we can work towards a more just and inclusive society.
