## Chapter: Chapter 11: Ethnicity and Education
### Introduction
In this chapter, we delve into the intricate relationship between ethnicity and education, a topic that has been the subject of extensive research and debate in the field of ethnic politics. The intersection of ethnicity and education is a complex one, with far-reaching implications for political systems, policy-making, and societal development.

Education is a fundamental human right and a cornerstone of social and economic development. It is also a potent tool for political socialization, shaping individuals' political attitudes, beliefs, and behaviors. However, the access to and quality of education can vary significantly across different ethnic groups, leading to disparities that can fuel social tensions and political conflicts.

Ethnicity, as a social construct, plays a significant role in shaping educational experiences and outcomes. It can influence the distribution of educational resources, the content of curricula, the language of instruction, and the nature of teacher-student interactions. These factors, in turn, can have profound effects on individuals' political socialization and their engagement with the political system.

In this chapter, we will explore these dynamics in detail, drawing on empirical research and theoretical insights from the fields of political science, sociology, and education. We will examine how ethnicity intersects with education at various levels - from individual classrooms to national education policies - and how these intersections can impact political processes and outcomes.

As we navigate through this complex terrain, we will also consider the potential of education as a tool for mitigating ethnic tensions and promoting social cohesion. We will look at various strategies and interventions that have been proposed or implemented in different contexts, and evaluate their effectiveness in addressing ethnic disparities in education and their implications for ethnic politics.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of the role of ethnicity in education and its political implications, contributing to a more nuanced understanding of ethnic politics. It is our hope that this exploration will not only enrich academic discourse but also inform policy-making and practice in education and ethnic politics.

### Section: 11.1 Ethnicity and Educational Inequality:
#### Subsection: 11.1a Ethnicity and Educational Access
The relationship between ethnicity and educational access is multifaceted and complex. It is influenced by a myriad of factors, including socio-economic status, immigration status, language proficiency, and cultural norms and values. These factors can create barriers to educational access for certain ethnic groups, leading to disparities in educational attainment.

In the United States, for instance, there are significant differences in educational attainment across different ethnic groups. According to data from the 1990s, Asian Americans had the highest educational attainment of any race, followed by whites. Hispanics or Latinos, on the other hand, had the lowest educational attainment, with less than 80% of the population graduating from high school[^1^].

These disparities can be partially explained by the influx of uneducated foreign-born Hispanic Americans who had not been offered the chance to complete secondary education in their home country and who had not completed secondary education in the United States[^1^]. However, this does not fully account for the disparities, as there are also significant differences in educational attainment within the U.S.-born population.

The disparities in educational attainment are also reflected in standardized test scores. In 2015, the average SAT scores on the math section were 598 for Asian-Americans, 534 for whites, 457 for Hispanic Latinos, and 428 for blacks[^1^]. These disparities in test scores can have significant implications for college admissions and, consequently, for individuals' future educational and career prospects.

These disparities in educational access and attainment can have far-reaching political implications. They can contribute to social and economic inequality, which can, in turn, fuel social tensions and political conflicts. They can also shape individuals' political attitudes and behaviors, influencing their political participation and their views on various political issues.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into these issues, examining the causes and consequences of ethnic disparities in education and exploring potential strategies for addressing these disparities.

[^1^]: Educational attainment in the United States. (n.d.). In Wikipedia. Retrieved from https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Educational_attainment_in_the_United_States

#### Subsection: 11.1b Ethnicity and Educational Achievement
The disparities in educational attainment across different ethnic groups are not only reflected in access to education but also in educational achievement. Educational achievement, often measured by standardized test scores, graduation rates, and college completion rates, can be significantly influenced by ethnicity.

As previously mentioned, in 2015, the average SAT scores on the math section were 598 for Asian-Americans, 534 for whites, 457 for Hispanic Latinos, and 428 for blacks[^1^]. This disparity in test scores is indicative of the broader disparities in educational achievement across different ethnic groups. 

Moreover, the percentage of individuals with a four-year college degree also varies significantly by ethnicity. Nearly half (49.8%) of Asian Americans, nearly a third (30%) of non-Hispanic whites, 17.3% of non-Hispanic Blacks, and just over a tenth (11.4%) of Hispanics or Latinos had a four-year college degree[^1^]. These disparities in college completion rates further underscore the impact of ethnicity on educational achievement.

The reasons for these disparities are complex and multifaceted. They can be attributed to a variety of factors, including socio-economic status, language proficiency, cultural norms and values, and the quality of education available to different ethnic groups. For instance, socio-economic status can influence educational achievement by affecting access to resources such as tutoring, test preparation services, and high-quality schools. Language proficiency can affect the ability of non-native English speakers to understand and engage with the curriculum. Cultural norms and values can influence attitudes towards education and academic achievement. The quality of education available to different ethnic groups can also play a significant role, with students in under-resourced schools often facing challenges that can hinder their educational achievement.

These disparities in educational achievement can have significant political implications. They can contribute to social and economic inequality, which can, in turn, exacerbate social tensions and political conflicts. They can also influence individuals' political attitudes and behaviors, shaping the political landscape in significant ways.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the political implications of these disparities in educational achievement, exploring how they can shape political attitudes, influence political participation, and contribute to political conflicts.

[^1^]: Educational attainment in the United States. (n.d.). In Wikipedia. Retrieved from https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Educational_attainment_in_the_United_States

#### Subsection: 11.1c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Educational Inequality
To further illustrate the impact of ethnicity on educational inequality, let's delve into some specific case studies. These cases will provide a more concrete understanding of how ethnicity can influence educational outcomes and opportunities.

##### Case Study 1: Native American Education in the United States
Native American students in the United States face unique challenges in the educational system. According to the National Indian Education Study, Native American students consistently perform lower than the national average in reading and mathematics[^2^]. This is partly due to the lack of resources and culturally relevant curriculum in schools serving Native American communities. 

Many Native American students attend schools in rural and isolated areas, which often struggle with a lack of funding, outdated materials, and insufficient staffing[^2^]. Additionally, the curriculum in these schools often fails to incorporate Native American history and culture, which can lead to disengagement and lower academic achievement[^2^].

##### Case Study 2: The Achievement Gap between African American and White Students
The achievement gap between African American and white students in the United States is a well-documented phenomenon. As mentioned earlier, African American students are more likely to be placed in special education and less likely to be diagnosed with ADHD, leading to more severe punishments and fewer learning opportunities[^1^]. 

Moreover, schools predominantly attended by African American students often have fewer resources and less experienced teachers, which can negatively impact the quality of education[^1^]. This systemic inequality contributes to lower test scores, graduation rates, and college completion rates among African American students compared to their white counterparts[^1^].

##### Case Study 3: Latino Education in the United States
Latino students in the United States also face significant educational disparities. Language barriers, socio-economic factors, and a lack of culturally relevant curriculum contribute to lower academic achievement among Latino students[^3^]. 

For instance, English Language Learners (ELLs), many of whom are Latino, often struggle with academic content due to language barriers[^3^]. Additionally, Latino students are more likely to attend under-resourced schools and have less access to advanced coursework, which can limit their educational opportunities[^3^].

These case studies highlight the pervasive impact of ethnicity on educational inequality. Addressing these disparities requires a multifaceted approach that includes increasing resources for under-resourced schools, implementing culturally relevant curriculum, and addressing systemic biases in the educational system.

[^2^]: National Indian Education Study. (2015). Performance of American Indian and Alaska Native Students at or above the proficient level. U.S. Department of Education.

[^3^]: Gándara, P., & Contreras, F. (2009). The Latino Education Crisis: The Consequences of Failed Social Policies. Harvard University Press.

#### Subsection: 11.2a Ethnicity and Educational Policy Making
Ethnicity plays a significant role in educational policy making, often influencing the allocation of resources, the development of curriculum, and the implementation of educational reforms. This influence can be seen in the way ethnic politics shape the educational landscape, particularly in multi-ethnic societies.

Ethnic politics, as defined by Chandra, can be understood as the political dynamics that arise from the interaction of different ethnic groups within a society[^3^]. These dynamics can manifest in various ways, such as through the formation of ethnic political parties or the mobilization of ethnic identities for political gain[^3^]. In the context of education, ethnic politics can influence policy making by shaping the priorities and agendas of policy makers, as well as the distribution of educational resources.

For instance, in a society where an ethnic political party is in power, educational policies may be designed to favor the ethnic group represented by the party. This could be done through the allocation of more resources to schools in regions predominantly inhabited by the ethnic group, or through the development of a curriculum that emphasizes the history and culture of the ethnic group[^3^]. Such policies, while potentially beneficial to the ethnic group in question, can exacerbate educational inequalities by disadvantaging other ethnic groups.

On the other hand, in a society where multiple ethnic groups coexist, educational policies may be used as a tool to promote social cohesion and integration. For example, a multi-ethnic curriculum could be developed to foster mutual understanding and respect among different ethnic groups[^4^]. However, the implementation of such policies can be challenging, particularly in societies with deep-seated ethnic tensions.

In addition to influencing the content of educational policies, ethnicity can also impact the process of policy making. For instance, ethnic politics can shape the negotiation and decision-making processes involved in the development of educational policies. In some cases, ethnic tensions can lead to policy gridlock, making it difficult to implement necessary educational reforms[^5^].

In conclusion, ethnicity plays a crucial role in educational policy making, influencing both the content and process of policy development. Understanding the interplay between ethnicity and educational policy is therefore essential for addressing educational inequalities and promoting educational equity.

[^3^]: Chandra, K. (2004). Why Ethnic Parties Succeed: Patronage and Ethnic Head Counts in India. Cambridge University Press.

[^4^]: Banks, J. A. (2004). Diversity and Citizenship Education: Global Perspectives. Jossey-Bass.

[^5^]: Lijphart, A. (1977). Democracy in Plural Societies: A Comparative Exploration. Yale University Press.

#### Subsection: 11.2b Ethnicity and Educational Reform
Ethnicity plays a crucial role in educational reform, often shaping the strategies and initiatives aimed at improving educational outcomes for different ethnic groups. This influence can be seen in the various institutional changes and school-based reforms implemented to address the racial achievement gap in the United States[^5^].

Institutional changes such as lowering class size in schools with a large population of minority students, expanding access to high-quality preschool programs to minority families, and focusing on teaching critical thinking and problem-solving skills have been shown to improve the performance of minority students[^5^]. These changes are often influenced by ethnic politics, as policy makers strive to address the educational needs of different ethnic groups.

School-based reforms have also been implemented to close the achievement gap. These include investments in pre-kindergarten programs, class size reduction, small schools, curricular reform, alignment of pre-kindergarten through college standards and expectations, and improved teacher education programs[^5^]. Many of these reforms are designed to provide minority students with the resources and support they need to succeed academically.

After-school activities such as tutoring sessions, remedial programs, and rapid assessment programs have been introduced in many schools to help minority students learn at a more accelerated rate[^5^]. These programs are often influenced by ethnic politics, as they are designed to address the specific educational challenges faced by different ethnic groups.

De-tracking, which involves providing the same quality education for all students regardless of race, has been implemented in some schools as a way to promote educational equality[^5^]. This reform is often influenced by ethnic politics, as it challenges the traditional educational structures that often disadvantage minority students.

Charter schools, which serve a fraction of the nation's public school students, have also played a prominent role in educational reform[^5^]. The effectiveness of charter schools in educating students is still open to debate, with research showing mixed results due to the complexities of comparison and wide performance differences among charters[^5^].

In conclusion, ethnicity plays a significant role in educational reform, influencing the strategies and initiatives aimed at improving educational outcomes for different ethnic groups. Understanding the impact of ethnicity on educational reform is crucial for developing effective policies and practices that promote educational equality and improve the academic performance of all students, regardless of their ethnic background.

[^5^]: "Racial achievement gap in the United States", Wikipedia, 2021.

#### Subsection: 11.2c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Educational Policy
In order to better understand the impact of ethnicity on educational policy, it is beneficial to examine specific case studies. Two such examples are the Race Equality in Education report from England and the Race Equality and Education resource from the Association of Teachers and Lecturers (ATL) in the UK.

##### Case Study 1: Race Equality in Education - England
The Race Equality in Education report was a survey conducted by local authorities in England between the summer of 2003 and the spring of 2005[^6^]. The survey focused on schools and local education authorities (LEAs) that were effectively involved in race equality in education. Four areas were examined by inspectors: improving standards and achievement amongst groups of pupils, with reference to the Race Relations (Amendment) Act 2000 (RRAA); the incorporation of race equality concepts into the curriculum in schools; the handling and reporting of race-related incidents in schools; and the work of schools and LEAs in improving links with local minority ethnic communities[^6^].

The report found that schools and LEAs that were effectively involved in race equality in education had implemented policies and practices that promoted racial equality and addressed issues of racism. These included incorporating race equality concepts into the curriculum, handling and reporting race-related incidents in a timely and effective manner, and working to improve links with local minority ethnic communities[^6^].

##### Case Study 2: Race Equality and Education – UK Educational System

The Association of Teachers and Lecturers (ATL) in the UK introduced a practical resource for the school workforce titled Race Equality and Education. This publication aimed to examine the racial, religious, or cultural terminology regularly used in today's society, in an attempt to combat prejudice based on color, ethnicity, and religion[^7^].

The resource provided practical advice and guidance for teachers and other education professionals on how to promote race equality and tackle racism in schools. It also provided information on the legal responsibilities of schools and teachers in relation to race equality, and offered practical strategies for promoting race equality and tackling racism in the classroom[^7^].

These case studies highlight the importance of educational policies that promote race equality and address issues of racism. They also underscore the role of ethnic politics in shaping these policies, as well as the need for ongoing efforts to ensure that all students, regardless of their ethnic background, have access to a high-quality education.

[^6^]: Race equality in education - a survey report by England. (2005). Her Majesty's Inspectors (HMIs).

[^7^]: Race equality and education – by UK educational system. (n.d.). The Association of Teachers and Lecturers (ATL).

### Section: 11.3 Ethnicity and Educational Attitudes:
#### Subsection: 11.3a Ethnicity and Attitudes towards Education
Understanding the role of ethnicity in shaping attitudes towards education is crucial in the broader context of ethnic politics. The Integrated Threat Theory (ITT) provides a useful framework for understanding how perceived threats, both realistic and symbolic, can influence attitudes and behaviors[^8^].

In the context of education, realistic threats may arise from perceptions of competition for educational resources, such as access to quality schools, scholarships, or academic opportunities. For instance, majority ethnic groups may perceive minority groups as a threat if they believe that the latter's educational advancement will limit their own opportunities. This is similar to the study by Esses et al. (1998), where participants who perceived immigrants as an economic threat had less favorable attitudes towards them[^9^].

Symbolic threats, on the other hand, are related to perceived threats to one's cultural identity and values. In the educational context, this could manifest in resistance to the inclusion of minority ethnic histories, languages, or perspectives in the curriculum. Majority ethnic groups may perceive such inclusion as a threat to their cultural dominance or the status quo, leading to negative attitudes towards such educational policies[^10^].

However, it is important to note that these attitudes are not static and can be influenced by various factors, such as intergroup contact, education, and exposure to different perspectives. For instance, Allport's Contact Hypothesis suggests that positive, meaningful contact between different ethnic groups can reduce prejudice and improve attitudes[^11^].

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the impact of ethnicity on educational attitudes, exploring both the challenges and potential solutions to these issues.

[^8^]: Stephan, W. G., & Renfro, C. L. (2002). The role of threat in intergroup relations. From prejudice to intergroup emotions: Differentiated reactions to social groups, 191-207.

[^9^]: Esses, V. M., Dovidio, J. F., Jackson, L. M., & Armstrong, T. L. (2001). The immigration dilemma: The role of perceived group competition, ethnic prejudice, and national identity. Journal of Social issues, 57(3), 389-412.

[^10^]: Verkuyten, M. (2005). Ethnic group identification and group evaluation among minority and majority groups: Testing the multiculturalism hypothesis. Journal of personality and social psychology, 88(1), 121.

[^11^]: Allport, G. W. (1954). The nature of prejudice. Reading, MA: Addison-Wesley.

#### Subsection: 11.3b Ethnicity and Attitudes towards Educational Institutions
Ethnicity can also play a significant role in shaping attitudes towards educational institutions. This is particularly evident in multicultural societies, where educational institutions often serve as microcosms of the broader societal dynamics[^12^].

In the United Kingdom, for instance, attitudes towards multiculturalism have fluctuated over the years, reflecting changing societal perceptions and attitudes towards ethnic diversity[^13^]. These attitudes can, in turn, influence perceptions of and attitudes towards educational institutions, particularly those that strive to promote multiculturalism and diversity within their student bodies and curricula.

For example, a poll conducted by MORI for the BBC in 2005 found that 62 per cent of respondents agreed that multiculturalism made the UK a better place to live, compared to 32 percent who saw it as a threat[^13^]. However, by 2008, only 30 per cent saw multiculturalism as making the UK a better place to live, with 38 per cent seeing it as a threat[^13^]. This shift in attitudes towards multiculturalism could potentially impact attitudes towards educational institutions that promote multicultural values and practices.

Moreover, the Integrated Threat Theory (ITT) can also be applied to understand attitudes towards educational institutions. Realistic threats may arise from perceptions of competition for resources within educational institutions, such as access to quality teachers, facilities, or academic programs. Symbolic threats, on the other hand, may arise from perceived threats to one's cultural identity and values within the educational institution, such as the inclusion of minority ethnic histories, languages, or perspectives in the curriculum[^8^].

However, it is important to note that these attitudes are not static and can be influenced by various factors. For instance, research suggests that positive, meaningful contact between different ethnic groups within educational institutions can reduce prejudice and improve attitudes[^11^]. Therefore, educational institutions can play a crucial role in fostering positive intergroup relations and promoting more inclusive attitudes.

In the next section, we will explore the impact of ethnicity on educational outcomes, shedding light on the complex interplay between ethnicity, education, and politics.

[^8^]: Stephan, W. G., & Renfro, C. L. (2002). The role of threats in the racial attitudes of Blacks and Whites. Personality and Social Psychology Bulletin, 28(9), 1242-1254.

[^11^]: Allport, G. W. (1954). The nature of prejudice. Reading, MA: Addison-Wesley.

[^12^]: Banks, J. A. (2004). Multicultural education: Characteristics and goals. In J. A. Banks & C. A. M. Banks (Eds.), Handbook of research on multicultural education (2nd ed., pp. 3-29). San Francisco: Jossey-Bass.

[^13^]: Ipsos MORI. (2008). Attitudes to multiculturalism. Retrieved from https://www.ipsos.com/ipsos-mori/en-uk/attitudes-multiculturalism.

#### Subsection: 11.3c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Educational Attitudes
To further illustrate the impact of ethnicity on educational attitudes, let's consider two case studies: one from the United States and another from South Africa.

##### Case Study 1: The United States
In the United States, the impact of ethnicity on educational attitudes can be seen in the ongoing debates about affirmative action in higher education[^14^]. Affirmative action policies aim to increase representation of underrepresented ethnic groups in colleges and universities, often through preferential admissions policies[^14^].

However, these policies have been met with resistance from those who perceive them as a form of reverse discrimination, creating a realistic threat to their own access to higher education[^14^]. This is a clear example of how the Integrated Threat Theory can be applied to understand attitudes towards educational policies.

On the other hand, proponents of affirmative action argue that these policies are necessary to redress historical injustices and promote diversity in educational institutions[^15^]. They perceive the lack of diversity in higher education as a symbolic threat to the values of equality and social justice[^15^].

##### Case Study 2: South Africa
In South Africa, the legacy of apartheid continues to shape attitudes towards education. Despite the end of apartheid over two decades ago, educational institutions in South Africa remain largely segregated along racial lines[^16^].

Many black South Africans perceive the lack of access to quality education as a realistic threat to their economic prospects[^16^]. At the same time, the dominance of Western perspectives in the curriculum is seen as a symbolic threat to their cultural identity[^16^].

Efforts to reform the education system have been met with resistance from those who perceive these changes as a threat to their own access to quality education[^17^]. This resistance can be understood in terms of the Integrated Threat Theory, with perceived threats leading to prejudice and resistance to change[^17^].

These case studies illustrate how ethnicity can shape attitudes towards education in different contexts. They also highlight the importance of understanding the underlying threats that drive these attitudes, as proposed by the Integrated Threat Theory.

In the next section, we will explore strategies for addressing these threats and promoting positive attitudes towards diversity in education.

[^14^]: Bowen, W. G., & Bok, D. (1998). The Shape of the River: Long-term Consequences of Considering Race in College and University Admissions. Princeton University Press.

[^15^]: Gurin, P., Dey, E. L., Hurtado, S., & Gurin, G. (2002). Diversity and Higher Education: Theory and Impact on Educational Outcomes. Harvard Educational Review, 72(3), 330-366.

[^16^]: Soudien, C. (2004). 'Constituting the class': An analysis of the process of 'integration' in South African schools. In L. Chisholm (Ed.), Changing class: Education and social change in post-apartheid South Africa (pp. 89-114). HSRC Press.

[^17^]: Jansen, J. D. (2004). Autonomy and accountability in the regulation of the teaching profession: A South African case study. Research Papers in Education, 19(1), 51-66.

### Conclusion
Throughout this chapter, we have delved into the complex relationship between ethnicity and education, and how it impacts politics. We have seen that ethnicity can influence educational opportunities and outcomes, which in turn can shape political attitudes and behaviors. Ethnicity can also be a source of political mobilization, as groups seek to advance their interests through the educational system. 

We have also explored how the educational system can be a site of ethnic conflict, as different groups vie for resources and recognition. Yet, it can also be a space for interethnic understanding and cooperation, as individuals from different ethnic backgrounds interact and learn from each other. 

In conclusion, ethnicity and education are deeply intertwined, and their interplay has significant implications for politics. Understanding this relationship is crucial for policymakers, educators, and anyone interested in promoting social justice and political stability.

## Chapter: Ethnicity and Health
### Introduction
In the realm of ethnic politics, the intersection of ethnicity and health is a critical area of study. This chapter, "Ethnicity and Health," aims to delve into the complex relationship between these two factors and their implications on the political landscape. 

Health disparities among different ethnic groups are a global phenomenon, and these disparities often have profound political implications. The health of a population is not only a reflection of its biological and environmental factors but also a mirror of its social, economic, and political conditions. Ethnicity, as a social construct, plays a significant role in shaping these conditions. 

In this chapter, we will explore how ethnicity influences health outcomes and how these health disparities, in turn, impact politics. We will examine the role of ethnicity in shaping health policies, the political response to health disparities among different ethnic groups, and how these disparities can influence political behavior and attitudes. 

We will also delve into the complex interplay between ethnicity, health, and politics, exploring how they influence each other in a cyclical manner. For instance, how health disparities among different ethnic groups can lead to political mobilization for better health policies, which in turn can influence health outcomes. 

This chapter will provide a comprehensive understanding of the intricate relationship between ethnicity and health, and its impact on politics. It will shed light on the importance of addressing health disparities among different ethnic groups in order to achieve political stability and social justice. 

As we navigate through this chapter, it is important to remember that the relationship between ethnicity and health is multifaceted and complex, influenced by a myriad of factors. Therefore, any attempt to understand this relationship must take into account the broader social, economic, and political context.

### Section: 12.1 Ethnicity and Health Inequality:
Health inequality is a global issue that is deeply intertwined with ethnicity. Ethnicity, as a social construct, plays a significant role in shaping health outcomes. This section will delve into the complex relationship between ethnicity and health inequality, and how it impacts politics.

#### 12.1a Ethnicity and Health Access
Access to health services is a fundamental human right. However, it is a right that is often denied to certain ethnic groups due to structural inequalities. These inequalities are often rooted in systemic racism, discrimination, and socio-economic disparities, which can limit access to quality healthcare for these groups.

In the United States, for instance, the establishment of health policies such as Medicare and Medicaid in 1965 was an attempt to extend health insurance to a greater portion of the population, including those from vulnerable ethnic groups. Medicare is a federally funded program that provides health insurance for people aged 65 or older, people younger than 65 with certain disabilities, and people of any age who have End-Stage Renal Disease (ERSD). Medicaid, on the other hand, provides health coverage to certain low-income people and families and is largely state-governed.

However, studies have shown that for-profit hospitals tend to make healthcare less accessible to uninsured patients in addition to those under Medicaid in an effort to contain costs. This has a disproportionate impact on ethnic minorities, who are more likely to be uninsured or underinsured. 

The State Children's Health Insurance Program (SCHIP) is another program that provides low-cost health insurance to children in families who do not qualify for Medicaid but cannot afford private health insurance on their own. However, access to these programs can be limited for certain ethnic groups due to factors such as language barriers, lack of information, and fear of discrimination.

The necessity of achieving equity in quality of and access to healthcare is glaring and urgent. According to Fein (1972), this goal could include equal health outcomes for all by income group, equal expenditures per capita across income groups, or eliminating income as a healthcare rationing device. Some have proposed that a national health insurance plan with comprehensive benefits and no deductibles or other costs from the patients would provide the most equity. 

Fein also stressed that healthcare reform was needed, specifically in eliminating financial assistance to treat patients that depended on patient income or the quantity of services given. He proposed instead paying physicians on a salaried basis. This could potentially reduce the financial barriers to healthcare access for ethnic minorities.

In conclusion, ethnicity plays a significant role in health access. Addressing health disparities among different ethnic groups is not only a matter of social justice but also a political necessity. As we move forward, it is crucial to develop and implement policies that ensure equal access to quality healthcare for all, regardless of ethnicity.

#### 12.1b Ethnicity and Health Outcomes
Ethnicity can significantly influence health outcomes, with certain ethnic groups experiencing higher rates of specific diseases and health conditions. This is particularly evident in the African American community, which has been shown to suffer notable health disparities compared to their White peers. 

For instance, African Americans have higher rates of morbidity due to stroke, perinatal disease, and diabetes mellitus compared to Whites[^1^]. They also suffer high rates of colorectal, pancreatic, and stomach cancers[^2^]. In terms of mental health, African Americans report lower rates of overall life satisfaction, as well as heightened depressive symptoms and substance abuse compared to Whites[^3^].

These health disparities can be linked to distal stressors such as perceived prejudice and discrimination. One study showed that perceived prejudice was associated with irregular blood pressure throughout the day, which has been linked to long-term cardiovascular disease[^4^]. Exposure to racial prejudice has also been linked to negative health behaviors, such as smoking and substance abuse, which are associated with poor cardiovascular health[^5^]. 

A 2009 meta-analysis of 36 empirical studies revealed consistent effects of prejudice and discrimination on physical health (e.g., cardiovascular disease, hypertension, diabetes) among racial minorities[^6^]. That same review revealed that racial prejudice and discrimination were related to depressive symptoms and psychiatric distress in 110 empirical studies[^7^]. 

Proximal stressors, such as a sense of inferiority and low self-worth due to experiences with prejudice, have also been linked to emotional distress and psychiatric symptoms among African Americans[^8^]. 

These health disparities among ethnic groups have significant implications for politics. They highlight the need for policies that address systemic racism and discrimination in healthcare, as well as the social determinants of health. 

In the next section, we will delve into the political implications of ethnicity and health, and how they shape health policies and interventions.

[^1^]: Williams, D. R., & Mohammed, S. A. (2009). Discrimination and racial disparities in health: evidence and needed research. Journal of behavioral medicine, 32(1), 20-47.

[^2^]: American Cancer Society. (2016). Cancer Facts & Figures for African Americans 2016-2018. Atlanta: American Cancer Society.

[^3^]: Keyes, C. L. (2009). The Black-White paradox in health: Flourishing in the face of social inequality and discrimination. Journal of personality, 77(6), 1677-1706.

[^4^]: Steffen, P. R., & Bowden, M. (2006). Sleep disturbance mediates the relationship between perceived racism and depressive symptoms. Ethnicity & disease, 16(1), 16-21.

[^5^]: Borrell, L. N., Jacobs Jr, D. R., Williams, D. R., Pletcher, M. J., Houston, T. K., & Kiefe, C. I. (2007). Self-reported racial discrimination and substance use in the Coronary Artery Risk Development in Adults Study. American journal of epidemiology, 166(9), 1068-1079.

[^6^]: Pascoe, E. A., & Smart Richman, L. (2009). Perceived discrimination and health: a meta-analytic review. Psychological bulletin, 135(4), 531.

[^7^]: Ibid.

[^8^]: Williams, D. R., & Williams-Morris, R. (2000). Racism and mental health: the African American experience. Ethnicity & Health, 5(3-4), 243-268.

#### 12.1c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Health Inequality
In this section, we will delve into specific case studies that highlight the impact of ethnicity on health inequality. These case studies will provide a more in-depth understanding of the complex interplay between ethnicity, health, and politics.

##### Case Study 1: Native Americans and Health Inequality
Native Americans have historically faced significant health disparities compared to other ethnic groups in the United States[^9^]. For instance, they have higher rates of diabetes, alcoholism, and suicide compared to the national average[^10^]. These health disparities can be attributed to a variety of factors, including socioeconomic status, access to healthcare, and historical trauma[^11^].

The Indian Health Service (IHS), a federal agency responsible for providing healthcare to Native Americans, has been criticized for its inadequate funding and services[^12^]. Despite the establishment of the IHS, Native Americans continue to face significant barriers to healthcare, including long travel distances to IHS facilities, lack of insurance, and cultural insensitivity[^13^].

##### Case Study 2: Hispanic Americans and Health Inequality
Hispanic Americans, particularly those who are uninsured or have low income, also face significant health disparities. They have higher rates of obesity, diabetes, and liver disease compared to non-Hispanic Whites[^14^]. These health disparities can be attributed to factors such as language barriers, lack of access to preventive care, and discrimination[^15^].

Despite the implementation of the Affordable Care Act (ACA), which aimed to increase health insurance coverage among uninsured Americans, many Hispanic Americans remain uninsured. This is due to factors such as immigration status, lack of awareness about the ACA, and cost barriers[^16^].

These case studies underscore the need for health policies that address the unique health needs and challenges faced by different ethnic groups. They also highlight the importance of addressing structural inequalities, such as access to healthcare and socioeconomic status, in order to achieve health equity.

[^9^]: Indian Health Service. (2019). Disparities. Retrieved from https://www.ihs.gov/newsroom/factsheets/disparities/

[^10^]: Centers for Disease Control and Prevention. (2018). Health of American Indian or Alaska Native Population. Retrieved from https://www.cdc.gov/nchs/fastats/american-indian-health.htm

[^11^]: Warne, D., & Frizzell, L. B. (2014). American Indian health policy: historical trends and contemporary issues. American Journal of Public Health, 104(S3), S263-S267.

[^12^]: Indian Health Service. (2019). IHS Profile. Retrieved from https://www.ihs.gov/newsroom/factsheets/ihsprofile/

[^13^]: Sequist, T. D., Cullen, T., & Acton, K. J. (2011). Indian health service innovations have helped reduce health disparities affecting American Indian and Alaska Native people. Health Affairs, 30(10), 1965-1973.

[^14^]: Centers for Disease Control and Prevention. (2015). Hispanic Health. Retrieved from https://www.cdc.gov/vitalsigns/hispanic-health/index.html

[^15^]: Ortega, A. N., Rodriguez, H. P., & Vargas Bustamante, A. (2015). Policy dilemmas in Latino health care and implementation of the Affordable Care Act. Annual Review of Public Health, 36, 525-544.

[^16^]: Artiga, S., & Ubri, P. (2017). Living in an Immigrant Family in America: How Fear and Toxic Stress are Affecting Daily Life, Well-Being, & Health. Kaiser Family Foundation. Retrieved from https://www.kff.org/disparities-policy/issue-brief/living-in-an-immigrant-family-in-america-how-fear-and-toxic-stress-are-affecting-daily-life-well-being-health/

#### 12.2a Ethnicity and Health Policy Making
Ethnicity plays a significant role in health policy making, particularly in countries with diverse ethnic populations such as Kenya. The ethnic composition of a country can influence the distribution of health resources, the accessibility of healthcare services, and the overall health outcomes of different ethnic groups[^17^].

In Kenya, the political power held by the ethnic majority often leads to an uneven distribution of public resources, including healthcare[^18^]. This ethnic favoritism can result in disparities in health outcomes, with co-ethnics of the political leader often having more opportunities to access healthcare services[^19^]. This can lead to underdevelopment of healthcare infrastructure in regions predominantly inhabited by minority ethnic groups, further exacerbating health inequalities[^20^].

Language barriers can also impact the accessibility and quality of healthcare services. In Kenya, the two officially recognized languages are English and Swahili. However, many Kenyans primarily speak their native or regional language, which can limit their ability to communicate effectively with healthcare providers who may not speak these languages[^21^]. This can lead to misunderstandings, misdiagnoses, and inadequate treatment, further contributing to health disparities[^22^].

Health policies need to take into account these ethnic and linguistic differences to ensure equitable access to healthcare services. This could involve increasing funding for healthcare infrastructure in underdeveloped regions, implementing language interpretation services in healthcare facilities, and promoting cultural sensitivity training for healthcare providers[^23^].

However, addressing these issues is not straightforward. It requires a nuanced understanding of the complex interplay between ethnicity, politics, and health. It also requires political will and commitment to tackle deeply entrenched ethnic favoritism and discrimination[^24^].

In the next section, we will explore specific strategies and interventions that have been implemented to address ethnic disparities in health, with a focus on the Kenyan context.

#### 12.2b Ethnicity and Health Reform
Ethnicity and health reform are intrinsically linked, as the effectiveness of health reform policies can be significantly influenced by the ethnic composition of a population[^24^]. This is particularly evident in countries with diverse ethnic populations, where health reform initiatives need to be tailored to address the specific health needs and challenges of different ethnic groups[^25^].

In the United States, for instance, the Affordable Care Act (ACA) was enacted in 2010 with the aim of expanding health insurance coverage and improving healthcare outcomes. However, studies have shown that despite the ACA's overall success in reducing the number of uninsured individuals, significant disparities persist among different ethnic groups[^26^]. For example, Hispanics and African Americans are still more likely to be uninsured compared to Whites[^27^].

These disparities can be attributed to a variety of factors, including language barriers, cultural differences, and socioeconomic inequalities[^28^]. For instance, individuals who primarily speak a language other than English may face difficulties in understanding health insurance options and accessing healthcare services[^29^]. Cultural beliefs and practices can also influence health behaviors and attitudes towards healthcare, potentially leading to lower utilization of preventive services and poorer health outcomes[^30^].

Socioeconomic inequalities, often intertwined with ethnicity, can further exacerbate health disparities. Lower-income individuals, who are disproportionately represented among ethnic minorities, often face financial barriers to accessing healthcare, even with insurance[^31^]. They may also live in areas with limited access to quality healthcare facilities and providers, further hindering their ability to receive adequate care[^32^].

Health reform policies need to address these ethnic disparities to achieve health equity. This could involve implementing strategies to increase health literacy among ethnic minority groups, providing culturally and linguistically appropriate healthcare services, and addressing socioeconomic inequalities through broader social and economic policies[^33^]. 

However, achieving health equity is a complex and multifaceted challenge that requires not only policy interventions, but also a commitment to addressing the underlying social determinants of health[^34^]. As Fein (1972) and Reynolds (1976) have argued, health reform should aim to eliminate income as a healthcare rationing device and improve access to healthcare for all, regardless of ethnicity or socioeconomic status[^35^].

[^24^]: Smith, D. B. (2016). Health care divided: Race and healing a nation. University of Michigan Press.

[^25^]: Lillie-Blanton, M., & Hoffman, C. (2005). The role of health insurance coverage in reducing racial/ethnic disparities in health care. Health affairs, 24(2), 398-408.

[^26^]: Artiga, S., Orgera, K., & Damico, A. (2020). Changes in Health Coverage by Race and Ethnicity since the ACA, 2010-2018. Kaiser Family Foundation.

#### 12.2c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Health Policy
In this section, we will delve into specific case studies that highlight the intersection of ethnicity and health policy. These case studies will provide a more nuanced understanding of how ethnicity can influence health outcomes and how health policies can be tailored to address these disparities.

##### Case Study 1: Mental Health in Hispanic/Latino Communities
The Hispanic/Latino community in the United States provides a compelling case study of the intersection of ethnicity and health policy. As mentioned in the previous section, this community faces significant mental health challenges, with 15% of the population having a diagnosable mental illness[^33^]. This translates to 8.9 million individuals suffering from a mental illness[^34^].

Several factors contribute to these disparities, including language barriers, socioeconomic inequalities, and limited access to health care services[^35^]. For instance, bilingual patients are often evaluated differently when interviewed in English as opposed to Spanish, leading to potential misdiagnoses or under-treatment[^36^]. Additionally, poor communication with health providers and low insurance coverage further exacerbate these disparities[^37^].

Health policies aimed at addressing these disparities need to take into account these unique challenges. For instance, policies could be implemented to increase the availability of bilingual mental health professionals and to improve communication between patients and providers[^38^]. Additionally, policies could aim to increase insurance coverage and access to mental health services for low-income individuals in this community[^39^].

##### Case Study 2: The Affordable Care Act and Ethnic Disparities
The Affordable Care Act (ACA) provides another important case study of the intersection of ethnicity and health policy. Despite the ACA's overall success in reducing the number of uninsured individuals, significant disparities persist among different ethnic groups[^40^]. For example, Hispanics and African Americans are still more likely to be uninsured compared to Whites[^41^].

These disparities can be attributed to a variety of factors, including language barriers, cultural differences, and socioeconomic inequalities[^42^]. Health reform policies, therefore, need to address these ethnic disparities to achieve health equity. This could involve implementing strategies to increase health literacy among non-English speakers, addressing cultural beliefs and practices that may hinder healthcare utilization, and reducing financial barriers to accessing healthcare[^43^].

In conclusion, these case studies highlight the complex interplay between ethnicity and health policy. They underscore the need for health policies to be tailored to the specific needs and challenges of different ethnic groups in order to achieve health equity.

### Section: 12.3 Ethnicity and Health Behaviors:
#### 12.3a Ethnicity and Health Behaviors
Ethnicity can significantly influence health behaviors, which in turn, can impact health outcomes. Health behaviors refer to actions taken by individuals that affect health or mortality. These can include behaviors like diet, physical activity, smoking, and alcohol consumption[^40^]. 

Research has shown that different ethnic groups have varying health behaviors, which can contribute to health disparities. For instance, studies have found that African Americans are more likely to have diets high in fat and low in fruits and vegetables compared to other ethnic groups[^41^]. This dietary pattern can contribute to higher rates of obesity and chronic diseases such as heart disease and diabetes among African Americans[^42^].

Similarly, Hispanic/Latino populations have been found to have higher rates of physical inactivity compared to non-Hispanic whites[^43^]. This lack of physical activity can contribute to higher rates of obesity and related health conditions in this population[^44^].

Understanding the role of ethnicity in health behaviors is crucial for developing effective health interventions. For instance, interventions aimed at promoting healthy eating and physical activity among African Americans and Hispanic/Latinos need to take into account cultural food preferences and barriers to physical activity in these communities[^45^].

However, it is important to note that ethnicity is not the sole determinant of health behaviors. Other factors such as socioeconomic status, education, and access to health care can also significantly influence health behaviors[^46^]. Therefore, a comprehensive approach that addresses these multiple factors is needed to effectively reduce health disparities.

In the next section, we will delve into the role of ethnicity in health care utilization and access, another critical aspect of health disparities.

#### 12.3b Ethnicity and Health Beliefs
Ethnicity can also significantly influence health beliefs, which in turn, can impact health behaviors and outcomes. Health beliefs refer to the perceptions and attitudes that individuals have about health, illness, and medical care[^47^]. These beliefs can be shaped by a variety of factors, including cultural traditions, religious beliefs, and historical experiences[^48^].

For instance, research has shown that some ethnic groups may have different beliefs about the causes of illness, the effectiveness of medical treatments, and the importance of preventive health behaviors. These beliefs can influence health behaviors such as medication adherence, use of preventive health services, and engagement in health-promoting behaviors[^49^].

For example, studies have found that some African American individuals may have a mistrust of the medical system due to historical experiences of discrimination and unethical medical practices, such as the Tuskegee Syphilis Study[^50^]. This mistrust can lead to lower rates of preventive health care utilization and poorer health outcomes[^51^].

Similarly, some Hispanic/Latino individuals may have cultural beliefs that emphasize the role of fate in health, which can lead to fatalistic attitudes towards chronic disease prevention and management[^52^]. These beliefs can contribute to lower rates of preventive health behaviors such as regular physical activity and healthy eating[^53^].

Understanding the role of ethnicity in health beliefs is crucial for developing culturally sensitive health interventions. Health education and communication strategies need to take into account the health beliefs of different ethnic groups to effectively promote health behaviors and improve health outcomes[^54^].

However, it is important to note that health beliefs are not static and can be influenced by a variety of factors, including acculturation, education, and exposure to health information[^55^]. Therefore, health interventions need to consider these factors when addressing health beliefs in different ethnic groups.

In the next section, we will explore the role of ethnicity in health care utilization and access, another critical aspect of health disparities.

#### 12.3c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Health Behaviors
In order to further understand the impact of ethnicity on health behaviors, it is beneficial to examine specific case studies. These studies provide a more detailed and nuanced understanding of the complex interplay between ethnicity, health beliefs, and health behaviors.

##### Case Study 1: African American Women and Breast Cancer Screening
A study by Peek, Sayad, and Markwardt (2008) examined the impact of cultural beliefs and attitudes on breast cancer screening behaviors among African American women[^56^]. The study found that cultural beliefs about breast cancer, including the belief that it is a death sentence and the fear of discovering cancer, were significant barriers to screening. Additionally, mistrust of the medical system and experiences of racial discrimination also negatively impacted screening behaviors[^56^]. These findings highlight the importance of addressing cultural beliefs and experiences of discrimination in interventions aimed at promoting breast cancer screening among African American women.

##### Case Study 2: Asian American Men and Cardiovascular Disease Prevention
A study by Ma, Yeh, and Leung (2018) explored the influence of cultural beliefs on cardiovascular disease prevention behaviors among Asian American men[^57^]. The study found that cultural beliefs about health and illness, such as the belief in maintaining balance and harmony, influenced dietary and physical activity behaviors. Additionally, the study found that language barriers and lack of culturally appropriate health information were significant barriers to engaging in preventive health behaviors[^57^]. These findings underscore the need for culturally sensitive health interventions that take into account the unique cultural beliefs and experiences of Asian American men.

##### Case Study 3: Hispanic/Latino Adolescents and Obesity Prevention
A study by Arredondo et al. (2017) investigated the role of cultural and familial factors in obesity prevention behaviors among Hispanic/Latino adolescents[^58^]. The study found that cultural beliefs about food and body size, as well as family norms and practices, significantly influenced dietary and physical activity behaviors. The study also found that acculturation and exposure to health information influenced health beliefs and behaviors[^58^]. These findings suggest that interventions aimed at preventing obesity among Hispanic/Latino adolescents need to address cultural and familial factors, as well as promote access to health information.

These case studies illustrate the complex ways in which ethnicity can influence health behaviors. They highlight the need for culturally sensitive health interventions that take into account the unique health beliefs and experiences of different ethnic groups. Furthermore, they underscore the importance of addressing structural barriers, such as discrimination and language barriers, in promoting health behaviors and improving health outcomes.

[^56^]: Peek, M. E., Sayad, J. V., & Markwardt, R. (2008). Fear, fatalism and breast cancer screening in low-income African-American women: the role of clinicians and the health care system. Journal of General Internal Medicine, 23(11), 1847–1853. https://doi.org/10.1007/s11606-008-0756-0

[^57^]: Ma, G. X., Yeh, M. C., & Leung, D. (2018). Cultural beliefs, self-efficacy, and health behaviors in Asian American men. American Journal of Men's Health, 12(5), 1312–1322. https://doi.org/10.1177/1557988318772391

[^58^]: Arredondo, E. M., Elder, J. P., Ayala, G. X., Campbell, N., Baquero, B., & Duerksen, S. (2005). Is parenting style related to children's healthy eating and physical activity in Latino families? Health Education Research, 20(6), 862–871. https://doi.org/10.1093/her/cyh014

### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have delved into the intricate relationship between ethnicity and health, and how it impacts politics. We have explored how ethnicity can influence health outcomes, and how these disparities can, in turn, shape political landscapes. The intersection of ethnicity and health is a complex one, with many factors at play, including socioeconomic status, access to healthcare, cultural beliefs, and systemic biases. 

We have also examined how these health disparities can lead to political action, as communities strive to address and rectify these inequalities. This can manifest in various ways, from grassroots activism to policy changes at the highest levels of government. The role of ethnicity in health and politics is a dynamic and evolving field, and it is crucial to continue examining and understanding these relationships to promote equity and justice.

In conclusion, the interplay between ethnicity, health, and politics is a multifaceted and complex issue. It is a topic that requires further research and understanding, as it has significant implications for policy-making, healthcare provision, and social justice. By continuing to explore this topic, we can work towards a more equitable and inclusive society.

## Chapter: Chapter 13: Ethnicity and Religion
### Introduction
In this chapter, we delve into the intricate relationship between ethnicity and religion, and how these two aspects of identity intertwine to shape political landscapes. The interplay between ethnicity and religion is a complex one, often resulting in unique political dynamics that can both unite and divide societies. 

Religion, like ethnicity, is a significant source of identity for many individuals and communities. It can serve as a unifying force, bringing together people of diverse ethnic backgrounds under a shared belief system. However, it can also accentuate ethnic differences, particularly when religious affiliations align with ethnic divisions. 

In the realm of politics, the intersection of ethnicity and religion can have profound implications. It can influence voting behavior, shape political alliances, and even fuel conflict. Understanding these dynamics is crucial for anyone seeking to navigate the often complex world of ethnic politics.

In the following sections, we will explore these themes in more detail, examining case studies from around the world to illustrate the multifaceted ways in which ethnicity and religion interact in the political sphere. We will also consider the implications of these interactions for policy-making and conflict resolution.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of the role of ethnicity and religion in politics, shedding light on the ways in which these two aspects of identity can both facilitate and hinder political cooperation. By the end of this chapter, readers should have a deeper understanding of the complexities of ethnic politics and the pivotal role that religion can play in shaping political outcomes.

### Section: 13.1 Ethnicity and Religious Identity:
#### 13.1a Ethnicity and Religious Affiliation
The relationship between ethnicity and religious affiliation is a complex one, often characterized by a high degree of overlap and interplay. Ethnicity, as a social construct, is often closely tied to religious beliefs and practices, with many ethnic groups sharing a common religious tradition. This is not to say that all members of an ethnic group necessarily adhere to the same religious beliefs, but rather that religion often plays a significant role in shaping ethnic identity and community.

Consider, for example, the demographics of Asian Americans. As one of the most ethnically diverse groups in the United States, Asian Americans exhibit a wide range of religious preferences. According to the Trinity College American Religious Identification Survey (ARIS) in 2008, no single religious affiliation claims a majority of Asian Americans. The survey found that 27% identified as none or agnostic, 21% identified with an Eastern religion, 17% identified as Catholic, 10% identified as generically Christian, 6% identified as mainline Protestant, 3% identified as Baptist, 2% identified as Pentecostal or other Protestant, and 8% identified as Muslim[^1^].

This diversity in religious affiliation among Asian Americans is reflective of the broader diversity within the Asian American community, which encompasses a wide range of ethnicities, cultures, and languages. It also underscores the complex relationship between ethnicity and religion, demonstrating how these two aspects of identity can intersect in multifaceted ways.

In the realm of politics, the intersection of ethnicity and religious affiliation can have profound implications. For instance, it can influence voting behavior, shape political alliances, and even fuel conflict. Understanding these dynamics is crucial for anyone seeking to navigate the often complex world of ethnic politics.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into these dynamics, examining how ethnicity and religious affiliation intersect in the political sphere and exploring the implications of these intersections for policy-making and conflict resolution.

[^1^]: Trinity College American Religious Identification Survey (ARIS), 2008.

#### 13.1b Ethnicity and Religious Practice
The relationship between ethnicity and religious practice is another facet of the intersection between ethnicity and religion. Religious practices, like religious beliefs, can be deeply intertwined with ethnic identity. They can serve as markers of ethnic identity, reinforce ethnic boundaries, and contribute to the maintenance of ethnic traditions and cultures.

Religious practices can vary widely within and across ethnic groups. For instance, within the Asian American community, religious practices can range from the congregational worship common in Christian denominations to the more individualized and less institutionalized practices associated with Eastern religions. According to a 2012 Pew Research Center survey, only 39 percent of Asian American households belong to a local church or temple[^2^]. This is due in part to the prevalence of atheism and adherence to Eastern religions without congregational traditions among Asian Americans.

The diversity of religious practices among Asian Americans is reflective of the broader diversity within the Asian American community. It also highlights the complex ways in which ethnicity and religion can intersect. For instance, religious practices can serve as a means of preserving ethnic traditions and cultures, reinforcing ethnic boundaries, and fostering a sense of ethnic identity and community.

In the realm of politics, the intersection of ethnicity and religious practice can have significant implications. For example, it can shape political attitudes and behaviors, influence policy preferences, and contribute to the formation of political alliances and divisions. Understanding these dynamics is essential for anyone seeking to navigate the complex world of ethnic politics.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the political implications of the intersection between ethnicity and religion, exploring how ethnicity and religious identity and practice can shape political behavior and influence political outcomes.

[^1^]: Trinity College American Religious Identification Survey (ARIS), 2008.

[^2^]: Pew Research Center survey of the Faiths of Asian Americans, 2012.

#### 13.1c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Religious Identity
In this section, we will explore case studies that highlight the intersection of ethnicity and religious identity, and how these factors can influence political attitudes, behaviors, and alliances. 

##### Case Study 1: Latino Catholics in the United States
Latino Catholics in the United States provide a compelling example of the intersection of ethnicity and religious identity. According to a 2014 Pew Research Center survey[^3^], 55 percent of Latinos in the United States identify as Catholic, making them a significant religious and ethnic minority. 

The religious identity of Latino Catholics is often deeply intertwined with their ethnic identity. For many, Catholicism is not just a religion, but also a cultural heritage that is passed down through generations. This strong religious identity can serve as a source of strength and resilience in the face of discrimination and marginalization[^4^].

In the political realm, Latino Catholics tend to lean Democratic, but they are not a monolithic voting bloc. Their political attitudes and behaviors are shaped by a variety of factors, including socioeconomic status, generational status, and the degree of their religious commitment[^5^].

##### Case Study 2: Muslim Immigrants in Europe
Muslim immigrants in Europe offer another illuminating case study. In countries like France and Germany, Muslim immigrants and their descendants constitute a significant ethnic and religious minority. Their religious identity often sets them apart from the majority population, and can be a source of both solidarity and tension[^6^].

In the political sphere, Muslim immigrants in Europe tend to be politically marginalized. They are underrepresented in political institutions and often face discrimination and prejudice. However, their religious identity can also serve as a mobilizing force, inspiring political activism and engagement[^7^].

These case studies underscore the complex ways in which ethnicity and religious identity can intersect and influence politics. They highlight the need for a nuanced understanding of these dynamics, taking into account not only the religious beliefs and practices of ethnic minorities, but also their social, economic, and political contexts.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the political implications of the intersection between ethnicity and religious identity, exploring how these factors can shape political attitudes, behaviors, and alliances.

[^4^]: Espinosa, G., Elizondo, V., & Miranda, J. (2003). Hispanic Churches in American Public Life: Summary of Findings. Retrieved from https://www.nd.edu/~latino/research/pdfs/hcapl_summary.pdf
