### Section: 13.2 Ethnicity and Religious Conflict:
#### 13.2a Ethnicity and Religious Violence
Religious violence, often intertwined with ethnic identities, has been a recurring theme throughout history. The Abrahamic religions, namely Judaism, Christianity, and Islam, have been particularly implicated in such conflicts[^8^]. Avalos argues that the sense of divine favor and superiority inherent in these religions can lead to violence, as these unverifiable claims cannot be objectively adjudicated[^9^]. Hickey echoes this sentiment, pointing to the long history of religious violence in the West, fueled by mutual antagonisms and struggles for survival against secular forces[^10^].

Schwartz takes this argument a step further, asserting that monotheistic religions, including Christianity, are inherently violent due to their exclusivism, which inevitably fosters violence against those considered outsiders[^11^]. This argument is not limited to the violent legacy of these religions but extends to the assertion that their legacy is genocidal in nature[^12^].

Christianity, for instance, did not develop the doctrine of "Holy war" until the 11th century. Before this, the Church propagated the pacifism of Jesus, and notable church fathers argued against joining the military or using any form of violence[^13^]. However, in the 4th century, St. Augustine developed a "Just War" concept, which allowed for limited uses of war to preserve peace and retain orthodoxy[^14^].

In the context of ethnicity, religious violence often takes on an additional layer of complexity. Ethnic groups may align themselves with particular religious identities, further deepening divisions and exacerbating conflicts. This intersection of ethnicity and religion can have profound implications for political attitudes, behaviors, and alliances, as demonstrated in the case studies of Latino Catholics in the United States and Muslim immigrants in Europe[^15^].

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the dynamics of ethnicity and religious conflict, exploring the underlying causes, manifestations, and potential solutions to these complex issues.

[^8^]: Avalos, H. (2005). Fighting Words: The Origins of Religious Violence. Prometheus Books.

[^9^]: Avalos, H. (2005). Fighting Words: The Origins of Religious Violence. Prometheus Books.

[^10^]: Hickey, E. W. (2003). Encyclopedia of Murder and Violent Crime. SAGE Publications.

[^11^]: Schwartz, R. M. (1997). The Curse of Cain: The Violent Legacy of Monotheism. University of Chicago Press.

[^12^]: Wechsler, L. (1997). Vermeer in Bosnia: Selected Writings. Pantheon Books.

[^13^]: Bainton, R. H. (1960). Christian Attitudes Toward War and Peace: A Historical Survey and Critical Re-evaluation. Abingdon Press.

[^14^]: Augustine of Hippo. (426 AD). The City of God. Book XIX, Chapter 7.

[^15^]: Pew Research Center. (2014). The Shifting Religious Identity of Latinos in the United States. Pew Research Center's Religion & Public Life Project.

#### 13.2b Ethnicity and Religious Tolerance
Religious tolerance, much like its counterpart religious violence, is often intertwined with ethnic identities. The concept of religious tolerance refers to the acceptance and respect for religious diversity, including beliefs and practices that differ from one's own[^16^]. This acceptance and respect can be seen in the context of individual attitudes, societal norms, and legal protections[^17^].

The case of Poland, as highlighted in the 2008 EVS survey, provides an interesting example of the interplay between ethnicity and religious tolerance. Despite experiencing a decrease in tolerance during the 1990s, Poland saw a marked increase in both political and ethnic tolerance from 1998 to 2008[^18^]. This increase in tolerance coincided with Poland's entry into the European Union, increased travel abroad, and more frequent encounters with people of other races[^19^].

The Roma group, often marginalized and discriminated against, was listed as the most intolerant group in the survey. This could be attributed to the group's historical experiences of exclusion and persecution, which may have fostered a sense of distrust and intolerance towards other ethnic and religious groups[^20^].

Religious tolerance, however, is not a static concept. It can fluctuate over time and across different contexts, influenced by a variety of factors including political climate, economic conditions, and societal attitudes[^21^]. For instance, the increase in tolerance in Southern Europe (Spain, Greece) and the decrease in other parts of Northern Europe (Netherlands) during the same time period as the EVS survey suggests that regional and national contexts play a significant role in shaping attitudes towards ethnicity and religion[^22^].

In the context of ethnicity, religious tolerance can have profound implications for political attitudes, behaviors, and alliances. Ethnic groups that align themselves with particular religious identities may foster a sense of unity and solidarity, which can contribute to more tolerant attitudes towards other ethnic and religious groups[^23^]. Conversely, ethnic groups that perceive themselves as being under threat from other religious groups may adopt less tolerant attitudes, as a form of self-preservation[^24^].

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the relationship between ethnicity and religious tolerance, exploring how these dynamics play out in different contexts and examining the implications for political behavior and policy-making.

#### 13.2c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Religious Conflict
The Bosnian War (1992-1995) provides a stark example of how ethnicity and religion can intertwine to fuel conflict. The war was primarily fought between three ethnic groups: the Bosniaks (Muslims), Croats (Catholics), and Serbs (Orthodox Christians)[^23^]. The conflict was characterized by ethnic cleansing, genocide, and the use of religious symbols for nationalist purposes[^24^].

The war was not solely a religious conflict, but religion played a significant role in shaping the identities of the warring factions and in justifying their actions. The religious leaders on all sides supported the war efforts and propagated views that further entrenched historical stereotypes and prejudices[^25^]. This use of religion for nationalist purposes transformed the Bosnian War into a conflict with features of a "religious war."

The Dayton Agreement, which ended the war in 1995, was intended to improve inter-ethnic relations. However, it has been argued that the agreement further segregated the country and damaged inter-ethnic relations[^26^]. The agreement divided Bosnia and Herzegovina into two entities, the Federation of Bosnia and Herzegovina (largely Bosniak and Croat) and the Republika Srpska (largely Serb), effectively institutionalizing the ethnic divisions that had fueled the war[^27^].

In the years following the war, all three ethnic groups experienced a drastic increase in the prevalence of ethno-nationalism, with the most dramatic shift being among the Serbs[^28^]. This increased ethno-nationalism contributed to the deterioration of inter-ethnic relations in the country.

The case of Bosnia and Herzegovina illustrates how ethnicity and religion can become intertwined in conflict, and how the aftermath of such conflicts can further entrench ethnic divisions and increase ethno-nationalism. It also highlights the potential pitfalls of peace agreements that institutionalize ethnic divisions, rather than promoting reconciliation and integration[^29^].

[^23^]: Burg, S. L., & Shoup, P. S. (1999). The war in Bosnia-Herzegovina: Ethnic conflict and international intervention. M.E. Sharpe.

[^24^]: Velikonja, M. (2003). Religious separation and political intolerance in Bosnia-Herzegovina. Texas A&M University Press.

[^25^]: Sells, M. A. (2002). The bridge betrayed: Religion and genocide in Bosnia. University of California Press.

[^26^]: Dyrstad, K. (2012). After ethnic civil war: Ethno-nationalism in the Western Balkans. Journal of Peace Research, 49(6), 817-831.

[^27^]: Bieber, F. (2006). Post-war Bosnia: Ethnicity, inequality and public sector governance. UNRISD.

[^28^]: Dyrstad, K. (2012). After ethnic civil war: Ethno-nationalism in the Western Balkans. Journal of Peace Research, 49(6), 817-831.

[^29^]: Bieber, F. (2006). Post-war Bosnia: Ethnicity, inequality and public sector governance. UNRISD.

### Section: 13.3 Ethnicity and Religious Institutions:
Religious institutions play a significant role in shaping the ethnic and religious identities of individuals and communities. They serve as a source of spiritual guidance, social support, and cultural preservation. However, the relationship between ethnicity and religious institutions is complex and multifaceted, often influenced by factors such as social identity, gender, and generational differences.

#### 13.3a Ethnicity and Religious Leadership
Religious leadership is a critical aspect of religious institutions. Leaders not only guide the spiritual journey of their followers but also play a significant role in shaping the ethnic identity of their communities. This is particularly true for ethnic minority communities, where religious leaders often serve as cultural ambassadors, preserving and promoting ethnic traditions and values within the context of their religious teachings[^29^].

According to Social Identity Theory, individuals from ethnic minority backgrounds may emphasize their religious identity as a means to maintain a positive self-concept when they feel their identity is threatened[^30^]. Religious leaders play a crucial role in this process by reinforcing the religious identity of their followers and providing a sense of belonging and acceptance. This is particularly evident among Latino and African American communities, where religious leaders often serve as advocates for social justice and equality[^31^].

Gender also plays a significant role in religious leadership. Generally, females are more likely than males to attend religious services and express that religion is an important aspect of their lives[^32^]. However, despite their active participation, women are often underrepresented in religious leadership positions. This gender disparity in religious leadership can impact the religious experiences and identities of female followers, potentially leading to feelings of marginalization and exclusion[^33^].

Generational differences also influence the relationship between ethnicity and religious leadership. First-generation immigrants often rely heavily on religious institutions and leaders for social support and cultural preservation. In contrast, second and third-generation immigrants may have a more complex relationship with religious institutions, balancing the traditions of their parents with the cultural norms of their adopted country[^34^].

In conclusion, the relationship between ethnicity and religious institutions is complex and multifaceted, influenced by a variety of factors including social identity, gender, and generational differences. Understanding these dynamics is crucial for understanding the role of religion in ethnic politics.

#### 13.3b Ethnicity and Religious Membership

Religious membership is another critical aspect of the relationship between ethnicity and religious institutions. The choice of religious affiliation often reflects an individual's ethnic background, cultural heritage, and personal beliefs[^33^]. This is evident in the diverse religious preferences of Asian Americans, which are influenced by the growth of Asian American immigration since 1965[^34^].

According to the Trinity College American Religious Identification Survey (ARIS) in 2008, no religious affiliation claims a majority of Asian Americans[^35^]. This diversity reflects the wide range of religious beliefs within the Asian American community, from Eastern religions to Christianity, Islam, and atheism. The Pew Research Center survey of the Faiths of Asian Americans in 2012 found that a plurality of Asian American respondents (42%) were Christian[^36^].

The relationship between ethnicity and religious membership is also evident in the Serbian community. In the 2022 census in Serbia, of those who declared as ethnic Serbs, 0.08% (4,238) declared Islam as their religion[^37^]. This indicates that while the majority of ethnic Serbs identify with the Orthodox Christian faith, a small but significant minority identifies with Islam.

Religious membership can serve as a source of social support and cultural preservation for ethnic communities. However, it can also lead to social exclusion and discrimination, particularly for minority religious groups. For instance, ethnic Serbs who identify as Muslim may face social stigma and discrimination due to their religious affiliation[^38^].

In conclusion, the relationship between ethnicity and religious membership is complex and multifaceted, shaped by factors such as immigration, cultural heritage, and social identity. Understanding this relationship is crucial for understanding the role of religious institutions in shaping ethnic identities and communities.

[^33^]: Smith, C., & Denton, M. L. (2005). Soul searching: The religious and spiritual lives of American teenagers. Oxford University Press.

[^34^]: Min, P. G. (2002). Religions of Asian Americans: An introduction. Walnut Creek, CA: AltaMira Press.

[^35^]: Kosmin, B. A., & Keysar, A. (2009). American Nones: The Profile of the No Religion Population. Trinity College.

[^36^]: Pew Research Center. (2012). The Rise of Asian Americans. Washington, D.C.: Pew Research Center.

[^37^]: Statistical Office of the Republic of Serbia. (2022). Census of Population, Households and Dwellings in the Republic of Serbia.

[^38^]: Perica, V. (2002). Balkan idols: Religion and nationalism in Yugoslav states. Oxford University Press.

#### 13.3c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Religious Institutions
In this section, we will explore case studies that highlight the complex relationship between ethnicity and religious institutions. These case studies will provide a more nuanced understanding of how ethnicity and religion intersect and influence each other.

##### Case Study 1: Latino Catholics in the United States
The Latino population in the United States is diverse, with roots in many different countries and cultures. However, a common thread among many Latinos is Catholicism. According to the Pew Research Center, 55% of Latinos in the United States identify as Catholic[^39^]. This religious affiliation is often tied to cultural heritage and identity, as Catholicism is a dominant religion in many Latin American countries.

However, the relationship between ethnicity and religious institutions is not static. Recent studies have shown a decline in Catholic affiliation among Latinos, particularly among younger generations[^40^]. This shift may be due to a variety of factors, including changing cultural norms, generational differences, and the influence of other religious institutions.

##### Case Study 2: The Sikh Community in India
The Sikh community in India provides another example of the intersection between ethnicity and religious institutions. Sikhism is a monotheistic religion that originated in the Punjab region of India in the 15th century. Today, the majority of Sikhs live in India, and the religion is closely tied to Punjabi culture and identity[^41^].

However, the relationship between Sikhism and Punjabi ethnicity is not straightforward. While most Sikhs are Punjabi, not all Punjabis are Sikh. Furthermore, there are significant Sikh populations outside of Punjab, including in the diaspora communities in the United States, Canada, and the United Kingdom[^42^]. These complexities highlight the multifaceted relationship between ethnicity and religious institutions.

In conclusion, these case studies illustrate the complex and dynamic relationship between ethnicity and religious institutions. They show that while ethnicity and religion are often closely linked, this relationship is influenced by a variety of factors, including cultural heritage, generational differences, and geographic location.

[^39^]: Pew Research Center. (2014). The Shifting Religious Identity of Latinos in the United States. Retrieved from https://www.pewforum.org/2014/05/07/the-shifting-religious-identity-of-latinos-in-the-united-states/

[^40^]: Ibid.

[^41^]: Singh, P. (2004). The Sikhs. New York: Knopf.

[^42^]: Ibid.

### Conclusion
In conclusion, the intersection of ethnicity and religion in politics is a complex and multifaceted issue. The influence of these two factors on political behavior and decision-making cannot be overstated. Ethnicity and religion often serve as powerful identity markers that can shape political affiliations, policy preferences, and voting behavior. They can also be instrumentalized by political actors to mobilize support, create divisions, or legitimize certain political agendas.

However, the relationship between ethnicity, religion, and politics is not static or uniform across different contexts. It can be shaped by a variety of factors, including historical experiences, socio-economic conditions, and institutional arrangements. Moreover, the political significance of ethnicity and religion can change over time, as societies evolve and new issues and identities emerge.

Understanding the dynamics of ethnic and religious politics, therefore, requires a nuanced and context-specific approach. It involves not only examining the role of ethnicity and religion in politics, but also understanding how they are shaped by and interact with other social, economic, and political factors. This understanding is crucial for promoting inclusive and democratic politics in ethnically and religiously diverse societies.

#### Exercise 2
Analyze how political actors can instrumentalize ethnicity and religion for political purposes. What are the potential consequences of this instrumentalization for social cohesion and political stability?

#### Exercise 3
Examine the factors that can influence the relationship between ethnicity, religion, and politics. How do these factors vary across different contexts?

#### Exercise 4
Reflect on the changes in the political significance of ethnicity and religion over time. What factors can contribute to these changes?

#### Exercise 5
Propose strategies for promoting inclusive and democratic politics in ethnically and religiously diverse societies. How can the potential negative impacts of ethnic and religious politics be mitigated?

## Chapter: Ethnicity and Gender
### Introduction
The intersection of ethnicity and gender in the political sphere is a complex and multifaceted topic. This chapter, "Ethnicity and Gender," aims to delve into this intricate subject, exploring how these two aspects of identity can influence and shape political landscapes, decisions, and outcomes. 

Ethnicity and gender, as social constructs, play significant roles in shaping individuals' experiences, opportunities, and challenges. In politics, these constructs can influence voting behavior, policy preferences, representation, and even the nature of political discourse. Understanding the interplay between ethnicity and gender in politics is crucial for fostering inclusive, equitable, and democratic societies.

This chapter will explore the various ways in which ethnicity and gender intersect in the political arena. It will examine how these intersections can both empower and marginalize certain groups, and how they can shape political ideologies, alliances, and conflicts. 

While this chapter does not claim to provide a comprehensive overview of all aspects of ethnicity and gender in politics, it aims to offer a solid foundation for understanding the key dynamics at play. It is hoped that this exploration will stimulate further discussion and research into this important and timely topic. 

In the absence of specific section topics, this chapter will be structured around key themes and case studies, drawing on a wide range of academic literature and empirical evidence. It will strive to present a balanced and nuanced perspective, acknowledging the diversity and complexity of experiences within and across different ethnic and gender groups. 

As we navigate through this chapter, it is important to keep in mind that the intersection of ethnicity and gender in politics is not a static or uniform phenomenon. It is shaped by a multitude of factors, including historical context, socio-economic conditions, cultural norms, and individual agency. Therefore, any analysis of this topic must be sensitive to these complexities and variations. 

In conclusion, "Ethnicity and Gender" promises to be an enlightening journey into the intricate world of ethnic politics, offering valuable insights into how these two dimensions of identity can shape and be shaped by political processes.

### Section: 14.1 Ethnicity and Gender Identity:
#### Subsection: 14.1a Ethnicity and Gender Roles
Ethnicity and gender are two fundamental aspects of identity that intersect in complex ways to shape individuals' experiences, opportunities, and challenges. This intersectionality is particularly evident in the realm of gender roles, which are culturally and socially constructed norms that dictate appropriate behaviors, activities, and expectations for individuals based on their perceived gender. 

In many societies, gender roles are closely tied to ethnicity, with different ethnic groups often having distinct gender norms and expectations. These ethnic-specific gender roles can have profound implications for individuals' identities, as well as for their social, economic, and political opportunities and challenges.

One illustrative example of the intersection of ethnicity and gender roles can be found in Native American societies. When European settlers first arrived in North America, they discovered that different Native American nations had unique concepts of sex and gender. For instance, many Native American societies recognized "Two-Spirits," individuals who embodied both masculine and feminine traits and often occupied special roles within their communities[^1^]. 

In the Navajo nation, for example, there were four recognized genders: man, woman, masculine female-bodied nádleeh, and feminine male-bodied nádleeh[^1^]. These gender roles were not rigidly tied to biological sex, and individuals were often assigned their gender roles based on their interests and behaviors from a young age[^1^]. 

The recognition and acceptance of gender diversity in these societies stands in stark contrast to the binary gender system prevalent in many Western societies, highlighting the significant influence of ethnicity on gender roles and identities. 

However, it is important to note that the intersection of ethnicity and gender is not a static or uniform phenomenon. It is shaped by a multitude of factors, including historical context, socio-economic conditions, and cultural norms. As such, the experiences and identities of individuals at this intersection can be incredibly diverse and complex.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the ways in which ethnicity and gender intersect to shape political landscapes, decisions, and outcomes. We will explore how these intersections can both empower and marginalize certain groups, and how they can shape political ideologies, alliances, and conflicts.

[^1^]: Roscoe, W. (1991). The Zuni Man-Woman. University of New Mexico Press.

#### Subsection: 14.1b Ethnicity and Gender Expression
Ethnicity and gender expression are two interconnected aspects of identity that significantly influence individuals' experiences and perceptions. Gender expression refers to the external manifestation of one's gender identity, which can be expressed through behavior, clothing, haircut, voice, and other physical characteristics[^2^]. It is important to note that gender expression can vary greatly across different ethnic groups, reflecting the diversity of cultural norms and expectations.

In the context of grammatical gender, as discussed in the related context, it is interesting to observe how language, a fundamental aspect of ethnicity, can influence gender expression. For instance, in languages with gendered nouns, such as Spanish and Portuguese, the gender of a noun can sometimes override the semantic meaning. This can lead to a form-meaning mismatch, where a noun that refers to a female person is grammatically masculine, or vice versa[^3^]. This linguistic phenomenon can subtly shape individuals' perceptions and expressions of gender, reinforcing certain gender norms and expectations within a given ethnic group.

Moreover, the intersection of ethnicity and gender expression can have significant implications for individuals' social, economic, and political opportunities and challenges. For instance, individuals whose gender expression does not conform to the dominant norms of their ethnic group may face discrimination and marginalization, limiting their access to resources and opportunities[^4^].

However, it is also important to note that the intersection of ethnicity and gender expression is not a static or uniform phenomenon. It is shaped by a multitude of factors, including historical, social, and political contexts, and can change over time. For instance, the acceptance of gender diversity in many Native American societies, as discussed in the previous section, stands in stark contrast to the binary gender system prevalent in many Western societies. This highlights the significant influence of ethnicity on gender expression, as well as the potential for change and diversity within and across ethnic groups[^1^].

In conclusion, understanding the intersection of ethnicity and gender expression is crucial for understanding the complex dynamics of identity, power, and inequality in contemporary societies. It provides valuable insights into the ways in which ethnicity and gender interact to shape individuals' experiences, opportunities, and challenges, and underscores the importance of promoting diversity and inclusion in all aspects of social, economic, and political life.

[^1^]: Roscoe, W. (1998). Changing Ones: Third and Fourth Genders in Native North America. New York: St. Martin's Press.

[^2^]: Serano, J. (2007). Whipping Girl: A Transsexual Woman on Sexism and the Scapegoating of Femininity. Emeryville, CA: Seal Press.

[^3^]: Corbett, G. G. (1991). Gender. Cambridge: Cambridge University Press.

[^4^]: Connell, R. (2009). Gender: In World Perspective. Cambridge: Polity Press.

#### Subsection: 14.1c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Gender Identity
The intersection of ethnicity and gender identity is a complex and multifaceted issue that is shaped by a multitude of factors, including cultural norms, societal expectations, and individual experiences. This section will explore this intersection through a series of case studies, focusing on the experiences of biracial and multiracial individuals.

##### Case Study 1: Biracial Identity and Gender
Biracial individuals, particularly those who identify as Black/White, often face unique challenges related to their racial and gender identities. As discussed in the related context, many biracial individuals feel the need to identify as one race due to societal pressures and discrimination[^5^]. This pressure can have significant implications for their psychological adjustment and self-esteem[^6^].

In terms of gender identity, biracial individuals may also face unique challenges. For instance, biracial women may experience a form of double discrimination, where they face bias and prejudice based on both their race and gender[^7^]. This can further complicate their identity development and psychological adjustment.

##### Case Study 2: Multiracial Identity and Gender
Multiracial individuals, like biracial individuals, often face challenges related to their racial and gender identities. However, these challenges can be even more complex due to the diversity of their racial backgrounds. For instance, multiracial individuals may face discrimination and bias from multiple racial groups, which can have significant implications for their psychological adjustment and self-esteem[^8^].

In terms of gender identity, multiracial individuals may also face unique challenges. For instance, multiracial individuals who identify as non-binary or transgender may face additional discrimination and bias, both within and outside their racial communities[^9^]. This can further complicate their identity development and psychological adjustment.

In conclusion, the intersection of ethnicity and gender identity is a complex and multifaceted issue that is shaped by a multitude of factors. By examining the experiences of biracial and multiracial individuals, we can gain a deeper understanding of this intersection and its implications for individuals' psychological adjustment and self-esteem. However, it is important to note that these experiences are not uniform and can vary greatly depending on individuals' specific racial backgrounds and gender identities.

#### Subsection: 14.2a Ethnicity and Gender Discrimination
Ethnicity and gender discrimination is a pervasive issue that intersects with various aspects of society, including politics. This form of discrimination is not only limited to the workplace but also permeates other areas such as education, healthcare, and housing. It is important to note that ethnicity and gender discrimination can manifest in both overt and subtle ways, and its impact can be profound and far-reaching[^10^].

##### Ethnicity and Gender Discrimination in the Workplace
In the workplace, ethnicity and gender discrimination can take on various forms. For instance, individuals from certain ethnic backgrounds may face bias in hiring practices, wage disparities, and limited opportunities for advancement[^11^]. Women, particularly those from marginalized ethnic groups, often face a "double bind" where they are discriminated against based on both their gender and ethnicity[^12^]. This can lead to lower wages, fewer promotions, and a higher likelihood of experiencing harassment or hostile work environments[^13^].

Title VII of the Civil Rights Act of 1964 and the Equal Employment Opportunity Act of 1972 provide some legal protections against such discrimination. However, enforcement can be challenging, and many instances of discrimination go unreported or unresolved[^14^].

##### Ethnicity and Gender Discrimination in Education
In the realm of education, ethnicity and gender discrimination can hinder access to quality education and educational resources. For instance, schools in ethnically diverse neighborhoods often receive less funding and have fewer resources compared to schools in predominantly white neighborhoods[^15^]. This can lead to disparities in educational outcomes and limit future opportunities for students from these communities[^16^].

Gender discrimination in education can also be a significant issue, particularly for girls from marginalized ethnic groups. They may face barriers to education due to cultural norms, societal expectations, and discrimination within the education system[^17^].

##### Ethnicity and Gender Discrimination in Healthcare
In healthcare, ethnicity and gender discrimination can lead to disparities in access to healthcare services, quality of care, and health outcomes. For instance, women from certain ethnic groups may face discrimination in the form of implicit bias from healthcare providers, leading to misdiagnosis or inadequate treatment[^18^]. They may also face barriers to accessing healthcare services due to language barriers, cultural misunderstandings, or lack of insurance[^19^].

In conclusion, ethnicity and gender discrimination is a complex issue that intersects with various aspects of society. It is crucial to continue researching and addressing this issue to promote equality and justice for all individuals, regardless of their ethnicity or gender.

#### Subsection: 14.2b Ethnicity and Gender Violence
Ethnicity and gender violence is a critical issue that intersects with politics, particularly in the context of marginalized communities. This form of violence is often exacerbated by systemic discrimination and social stigma, leading to higher rates of violence against certain ethnic and gender groups[^17^].

##### Ethnicity and Gender Violence: The Case of Transgender Women of Color
Transgender women of color in the United States experience violence at a significantly higher rate than their white transgender counterparts[^18^]. A study conducted by the Gender Public Advocacy Coalition found that of the 51 transgender murder victims under the age of 30 they analyzed from 1995 to 2005, 91% were people of color[^19^]. Another study revealed that 52% of the 51 transgender women of color surveyed had been raped[^20^].

Violence towards transgender women of color is often perpetrated by romantic or potential romantic partners, who may dehumanize these women based on harmful stereotypes[^21^]. These stereotypes, such as the assumption that transgender women of color are sex workers or substance abusers, are perpetuated by both straight and gay cisgender men[^22^].

Cisgender men in romantic relationships with transgender women of color often conceal their involvement, refusing to be seen with their partners in public or on social media[^23^]. This concealment is attributed to the social stigma surrounding transgender women of color, and can result in psychological trauma[^24^].

##### The Impact of Ethnicity and Gender Violence on Politics
The high rates of violence against transgender women of color and other marginalized groups have significant political implications. These include the need for policy changes to protect these communities, the mobilization of political activism, and the shaping of public discourse around gender and ethnicity[^25^].

For instance, the Violence Against Women Act (VAWA) in the United States, which provides legal protections for victims of domestic violence, does not explicitly include protections for transgender women[^26^]. This omission underscores the need for more inclusive policies that address the specific vulnerabilities of transgender women of color.

In conclusion, understanding the intersection of ethnicity and gender violence is crucial for creating effective policies and fostering a more inclusive society. It is essential to challenge harmful stereotypes, address systemic discrimination, and ensure that all individuals, regardless of their ethnicity or gender identity, are protected from violence.

[^17^]: Crenshaw, K. (1991). Mapping the Margins: Intersectionality, Identity Politics, and Violence against Women of Color. Stanford Law Review, 43(6), 1241-1299.

[^18^]: Gender Public Advocacy Coalition. (2005). 50 Under 30: Masculinity and the War on America's Youth. Washington, DC: GenderPAC.

[^19^]: Ibid.

[^20^]: Garofalo, R., Deleon, J., Osmer, E., Doll, M., & Harper, G. W. (2006). Overlooked, misunderstood and at-risk: Exploring the lives and HIV risk of ethnic minority male-to-female transgender youth. Journal of Adolescent Health, 38(3), 230-236.

[^21^]: Ibid.

[^22^]: Ibid.

[^23^]: Ibid.

[^24^]: Ibid.

[^25^]: Crenshaw, K. (1991). Mapping the Margins: Intersectionality, Identity Politics, and Violence against Women of Color. Stanford Law Review, 43(6), 1241-1299.

#### Subsection: 14.2c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Gender Inequality
##### Ethnicity and Gender Inequality in Technology: The Case of Women in India
In India, a country with a diverse ethnic population, gender inequality in technology is a significant issue. Despite the country's rapid technological advancement, women, particularly those from marginalized ethnic communities, are often left behind[^26^].

A study conducted by the International Center for Research on Women (ICRW) found that only 38% of women in India own mobile phones compared to 71% of men[^27^]. This digital divide is even more pronounced among women from marginalized ethnic communities, such as the Dalits and Adivasis[^28^].

The ICRW study also revealed that women's access to and use of technology is often controlled by men. For instance, many women reported that their husbands or fathers decide when and how they can use their mobile phones[^29^]. This control over women's use of technology is a form of gender inequality that is deeply rooted in patriarchal norms and practices[^30^].

The underrepresentation of women in technology and ICT-focused programs in India is another manifestation of gender inequality. Despite the government's efforts to promote women's participation in these fields, women remain a minority. This underrepresentation is often attributed to gender stereotypes and societal expectations that discourage women, particularly those from marginalized ethnic communities, from pursuing careers in technology[^31^].

##### The Impact of Ethnicity and Gender Inequality on Politics
The gender inequality in technology in India has significant political implications. It not only limits women's access to information and opportunities, but also their ability to participate in political processes[^32^].

For instance, the digital divide can prevent women, particularly those from marginalized ethnic communities, from accessing information about their political rights and participating in online political discussions[^33^]. This lack of access and participation can further marginalize these women and exacerbate existing inequalities[^34^].

The underrepresentation of women in technology and ICT-focused programs also has political implications. It can limit women's opportunities to influence policy decisions related to technology and contribute to the development of technological solutions that address their needs and concerns[^35^].

Therefore, addressing gender inequality in technology is not only a matter of social justice, but also a political imperative. It requires concerted efforts from the government, civil society, and the private sector to challenge patriarchal norms and practices, promote women's participation in technology, and ensure that technology serves as a tool for empowerment rather than exclusion[^36^].

#### Subsection: 14.3a Ethnicity and Gender Leadership
The intersection of ethnicity and gender in leadership roles is a complex and multifaceted issue. The leadership styles and effectiveness of individuals can be influenced by both their gender and their ethnic background. This section will explore how these two factors interact and impact leadership.

##### Gender Differences in Leadership
As mentioned in the related context, research has shown that there are statistically significant differences in the leadership styles of men and women. Women tend to adopt more participative and transformational leadership styles, emphasizing communication, cooperation, affiliation, and nurturing[^33^]. Men, on the other hand, are more likely to adopt directive and transactional leadership styles, focusing on goal and task orientation[^34^].

These differences in leadership styles can be attributed to societal expectations and gender norms. Women are often expected to be more communal and relationship-oriented, while men are expected to be more agentic and task-oriented[^35^]. However, it is important to note that these are general trends and there is considerable overlap between the leadership styles of men and women.

##### Ethnicity and Leadership
Ethnicity can also play a significant role in leadership. Research has shown that individuals from different ethnic backgrounds may have different leadership styles and approaches. For instance, a study by Cox and Blake in 1991 found that multicultural organizations are more effective at problem-solving, possess a broader range of perspectives, and are more innovative[^36^].

However, ethnicity can also present challenges in leadership. Individuals from minority ethnic backgrounds may face discrimination and bias, which can hinder their ability to lead effectively[^37^]. They may also face cultural barriers, such as language differences and differing social norms, which can impact their leadership style and effectiveness[^38^].

##### The Intersection of Ethnicity and Gender in Leadership
The intersection of ethnicity and gender in leadership can create unique challenges and opportunities. Women from minority ethnic backgrounds may face double discrimination due to their gender and ethnicity, which can hinder their leadership opportunities and effectiveness[^39^]. However, they may also bring unique perspectives and approaches to leadership, which can enhance organizational effectiveness[^40^].

In conclusion, both gender and ethnicity can significantly impact leadership styles and effectiveness. Understanding these impacts is crucial for promoting diversity and inclusion in leadership roles. Future research should continue to explore the intersection of ethnicity and gender in leadership, and how these factors can be leveraged to enhance leadership effectiveness and organizational performance.

[^33^]: Eagly, A. H., & Johnson, B. T. (1990). Gender and leadership style: A meta-analysis. Psychological Bulletin, 108(2), 233.

[^34^]: Trinidad, C., & Normore, A. H. (2005). Leadership and gender: A dangerous liaison?. Leadership & Organization Development Journal.

[^35^]: Yukl, G. (2002). Leadership in organizations. Prentice Hall.

[^36^]: Cox, T. H., & Blake, S. (1991). Managing cultural diversity: Implications for organizational competitiveness. The Executive, 5(3), 45-56.

[^37^]: Eagly, A. H., & Karau, S. J. (2002). Role congruity theory of prejudice toward female leaders. Psychological Review, 109(3), 573.

[^38^]: Cox, T. H., & Blake, S. (1991). Managing cultural diversity: Implications for organizational competitiveness. The Executive, 5(3), 45-56.

[^39^]: Nkomo, S. M. (1992). The emperor has no clothes: Rewriting “race in organizations”. Academy of Management Review, 17(3), 487-513.

[^40^]: Cox, T. H., & Blake, S. (1991). Managing cultural diversity: Implications for organizational competitiveness. The Executive, 5(3), 45-56.

#### Subsection: 14.3b Ethnicity and Gender Membership
The intersection of ethnicity and gender is not only evident in leadership roles but also in the membership of various social, political, and economic institutions. This section will delve into how ethnicity and gender influence membership in these institutions, with a particular focus on the concept of third gender and its recognition in different societies.

##### Third Gender and Ethnicity
The concept of third gender, as discussed in the related context, is a social category present in many different cultures that goes beyond the binary of male and female[^39^]. This concept is often intertwined with ethnicity, as the recognition and understanding of third gender identities can vary greatly between different ethnic groups.

For instance, in many North American indigenous cultures, the term "Two-Spirit" is used to describe individuals who embody both masculine and feminine spirits[^40^]. This term, which was created at an Indigenous lesbian and gay conference in 1990, is a modern umbrella term intended to replace the derogatory term "berdache" used by non-Native anthropologists[^41^].

##### Ethnicity, Gender, and Institutional Membership
The recognition of third gender identities can have significant implications for institutional membership. In societies where third gender identities are legally recognized, individuals who identify as third gender may have access to certain rights and protections that are not available in societies where these identities are not recognized[^42^].

However, the intersection of ethnicity and third gender can complicate this issue. For example, in societies where third gender identities are recognized but are predominantly associated with a particular ethnic group, individuals from other ethnic groups who identify as third gender may face additional barriers to recognition and acceptance[^43^].

Moreover, the intersection of ethnicity and gender can also influence the representation of different ethnic groups within institutions. For instance, ethnic minorities and women are often underrepresented in political institutions, which can limit their influence and participation in decision-making processes[^44^].

In conclusion, the intersection of ethnicity and gender can significantly impact institutional membership. Understanding these dynamics is crucial for promoting inclusivity and equality in all aspects of society.

[^39^]: Fausto-Sterling, A. (2000). Sexing the body: Gender politics and the construction of sexuality. New York: Basic Books.

[^40^]: Roscoe, W. (1998). Changing ones: Third and fourth genders in native North America. New York: St. Martin's Press.

[^41^]: Pember, M. A. (2016). "Two Spirits, One Heart, Five Genders". Indian Country Today.

[^42^]: Currah, P., & Minter, S. (2000). Unprincipled exclusions: The struggle to achieve judicial and legislative equality for transgender people. William and Mary Journal of Women and the Law, 7, 37.

[^43^]: Norton, J. (2013). Transgender identity and the socio-political inclusion of gender diversity. Transgender Studies Quarterly, 1(1), 34-45.

[^44^]: Hughes, M. M., & Paxton, P. (2008). Continuous change, episodes, and critical periods: A framework for understanding women's political representation over time. Politics & Gender, 4(2), 233-264.

#### 14.3c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Gender Institutions
In this section, we will explore case studies that highlight the intersection of ethnicity, gender, and institutional membership. These case studies will provide a deeper understanding of how ethnicity and gender, particularly third gender identities, can influence and shape institutional structures and practices.

##### Case Study 1: Legal Recognition of Third Gender in India
In 2014, India's Supreme Court recognized the Hijra community, a third gender group in South Asia, as a legal third gender[^44^]. This landmark ruling granted the Hijra community, which has a long history and cultural presence in South Asia, legal recognition and protection under the law[^45^]. 

However, despite this legal recognition, the Hijra community continues to face significant social and economic marginalization. This case study highlights the complexities of institutional recognition of third gender identities, particularly in societies where these identities are intertwined with specific ethnic or cultural groups[^46^].

##### Case Study 2: Two-Spirit Recognition in North American Indigenous Cultures
As previously discussed, the term "Two-Spirit" is used in many North American indigenous cultures to describe individuals who embody both masculine and feminine spirits[^47^]. This term is a modern umbrella term intended to replace the derogatory term "berdache" used by non-Native anthropologists[^48^].

However, the recognition and understanding of Two-Spirit identities can vary greatly between different indigenous communities, as well as between indigenous and non-indigenous communities[^49^]. This case study underscores the importance of understanding the specific cultural and ethnic contexts in which third gender identities are situated[^50^].

##### Case Study 3: Third Gender in Samoa
In Samoa, a Polynesian country, the fa'afafine are a recognized third-gender group. The fa'afafine, which translates to "in the manner of a woman," are assigned male at birth but take on female gender roles[^51^]. The fa'afafine are socially accepted and integrated into Samoan society, demonstrating a case where third gender recognition is not only institutional but also culturally embedded[^52^].

These case studies illustrate the diverse ways in which ethnicity and gender intersect within institutional contexts. They highlight the importance of cultural understanding and sensitivity when addressing issues related to third gender identities and their recognition within various societal institutions[^53^].

### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have delved into the intricate relationship between ethnicity and gender, and how these two factors interplay in the realm of politics. We have explored how ethnicity and gender can shape political landscapes, influence policy-making, and affect the representation and participation of different groups in political processes. 

We have also examined how the intersectionality of ethnicity and gender can compound the challenges faced by certain groups, particularly ethnic minority women. This intersectionality can lead to double marginalization, where individuals are disadvantaged due to their ethnic background and gender. 

Moreover, we have discussed how understanding the dynamics of ethnicity and gender in politics can help in formulating inclusive policies and promoting equitable political participation. It is crucial to acknowledge and address the unique experiences and challenges of different ethnic and gender groups to ensure a truly democratic and representative political system.

In conclusion, ethnicity and gender are not just personal identities but are also political categories that can significantly impact the political sphere. Recognizing and understanding these impacts is a crucial step towards achieving a more inclusive and equitable political landscape.

## Chapter: Chapter 15: Ethnicity and the Environment
### Introduction
In this chapter, we delve into the intricate relationship between ethnicity and the environment, a topic that is often overlooked in the discourse of ethnic politics. The environment, in this context, is not limited to the physical surroundings but also encompasses the socio-economic and political environment within which ethnic groups exist. 

Ethnicity, as a social construct, has profound implications on how different groups interact with their environment. These interactions can be seen in the way resources are managed, how environmental policies are formulated and implemented, and how environmental issues are perceived and addressed. 

The chapter will explore how ethnicity can influence environmental attitudes and behaviors, and how these in turn can shape political dynamics. We will also examine the role of ethnic politics in environmental governance and the potential for ethnic conflicts over environmental resources. 

While the relationship between ethnicity and the environment is complex and multifaceted, understanding this relationship is crucial for developing inclusive and sustainable environmental policies. This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive overview of this important aspect of ethnic politics, shedding light on the ways in which ethnicity and the environment intersect and influence each other. 

As we navigate through this chapter, we will strive to present a balanced view, acknowledging the potential for both conflict and cooperation in the intersection of ethnicity and the environment. We will also highlight the importance of considering ethnic diversity in environmental policy-making, and the potential benefits of incorporating traditional ecological knowledge held by different ethnic groups into modern environmental management strategies. 

In conclusion, this chapter will underscore the importance of understanding the interplay between ethnicity and the environment in the broader context of ethnic politics. It is our hope that this exploration will contribute to a more nuanced understanding of ethnic politics and its impact on our world.

### Section: 15.1 Ethnicity and Environmental Inequality
In this section, we will delve into the concept of environmental inequality and its relationship with ethnicity. Environmental inequality refers to the uneven distribution of environmental hazards and access to environmental goods, often along racial, ethnic, and socio-economic lines. This inequality manifests in various forms, such as differential exposure to pollution, unequal access to clean water and air, and disparities in the quality of public spaces like parks and playgrounds.

#### Subsection 15.1a Ethnicity and Environmental Access
Ethnicity plays a significant role in determining access to environmental goods. This is evident in the phenomenon of environmental racism, where minority communities are disproportionately targeted for the siting of polluting industries and toxic waste disposal sites. This results in these communities bearing a greater environmental and health risk than the society at large[^1^].

Environmental racism is not a new phenomenon. It stems from the environmental movement of the 1960s and 1970s, which was primarily led by the middle class and largely ignored the plight of poor people and people of color[^1^]. This historical oversight has had lasting impacts, with these communities continuing to face greater exposure to environmental hazards.

Policies related to redlining and urban decay can also act as a form of environmental racism. Urban minority communities may face environmental racism in the form of parks that are smaller, less accessible, and of poorer quality than those in more affluent or white areas in some cities[^1^]. This not only deprives these communities of recreational spaces but also indirectly affects their health as they have fewer opportunities for exercise.

Moreover, impoverished or underdeveloped communities are not only at greater risk of contracting illnesses from public areas and disposal sites, but they are also less likely to be located near a distinguished hospital or treatment center[^1^]. This further exacerbates the health disparities faced by these communities.

In conclusion, ethnicity plays a significant role in determining access to environmental goods and exposure to environmental hazards. Understanding this relationship is crucial for developing inclusive and sustainable environmental policies that address these disparities.

[^1^]: Bullard, R.D. (1993). Confronting Environmental Racism: Voices from the Grassroots. South End Press.

#### 15.1b Ethnicity and Environmental Outcomes
The outcomes of environmental inequality are far-reaching and disproportionately affect ethnic communities. The environmental hazards that these communities are exposed to can lead to a range of negative health outcomes, including respiratory diseases, cancer, and other chronic conditions[^2^]. 

For instance, the proximity of communities of color to pollution sources such as landfills, power plants, and incinerators can lead to a higher prevalence of disease. Studies have shown that exposure to pollution can result in a higher prevalence of asthma, heart disease, and other health conditions[^3^]. 

In addition to physical health risks, environmental racism can also have significant impacts on mental health. Living in close proximity to environmental hazards can cause psychological stress, which can lead to mental health disorders such as anxiety and depression[^4^]. 

Environmental racism also has economic implications. Empirical evidence suggests that environmental hazards negatively affect nearby property values, employment opportunities, and economic activities[^5^]. This can lead to a cycle of poverty, where communities of color are trapped in areas with poor environmental conditions and limited economic opportunities.

Moreover, the impact of natural disasters is often more severe in communities of color due to their location and lack of resources for disaster prevention and recovery. The extent of poverty within a region can often have a much stronger effect on the scale of a natural disaster's impact than the severity of the disaster itself[^6^]. 

In conclusion, ethnicity plays a significant role in determining environmental outcomes. The disproportionate exposure of communities of color to environmental hazards leads to a range of negative health, economic, and social outcomes. Addressing environmental racism is therefore not only a matter of environmental justice but also a crucial step towards achieving racial and social equality.

[^2^]: Mohai, P., Pellow, D., & Roberts, J. T. (2009). Environmental Justice. Annual Review of Environment and Resources, 34, 405–430. https://doi.org/10.1146/annurev-environ-082508-094348

[^3^]: Morello-Frosch, R., Zuk, M., Jerrett, M., Shamasunder, B., & Kyle, A. D. (2011). Understanding the Cumulative Impacts of Inequalities in Environmental Health: Implications for Policy. Health Affairs, 30(5), 879–887. https://doi.org/10.1377/hlthaff.2011.0153

[^4^]: Downey, L., & Van Willigen, M. (2005). Environmental Stressors: The Mental Health Impacts of Living Near Industrial Activity. Journal of Health and Social Behavior, 46(3), 289–305. https://doi.org/10.1177/002214650504600305

[^5^]: Ringquist, E. J. (2005). Assessing Evidence of Environmental Inequities: A Meta-Analysis. Journal of Policy Analysis and Management, 24(2), 223–247. https://doi.org/10.1002/pam.20088

[^6^]: Cutter, S. L., Boruff, B. J., & Shirley, W. L. (2003). Social Vulnerability to Environmental Hazards. Social Science Quarterly, 84(2), 242–261. https://doi.org/10.1111/1540-6237.8402002

#### 15.1c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Environmental Inequality
To further illustrate the impact of ethnicity on environmental inequality, let's examine two case studies: the Flint Water Crisis and the unequal distribution of urban green spaces.

##### Flint Water Crisis
The Flint Water Crisis is a prime example of environmental racism in the United States[^7^]. In 2014, the city of Flint, Michigan, switched its water source from Lake Huron to the Flint River in an attempt to save money. However, the water from the Flint River was highly corrosive and led to lead contamination in the city's water supply[^8^].

The majority of Flint's population is African American, and the city has a high poverty rate[^9^]. The decision to switch water sources was made without proper testing or treatment of the water, and the residents of Flint were exposed to lead-contaminated water for over a year before the issue was publicly acknowledged[^10^].

The Flint Water Crisis has had severe health and economic impacts on the city's residents. Lead exposure can lead to a range of health problems, including developmental issues in children, and the crisis has also led to a decrease in property values and economic activity in the city[^11^].

##### Urban Green Spaces
Urban green spaces, such as parks and community gardens, can have significant health and social benefits. However, access to these spaces is often unequal, with communities of color and low-income communities having less access to green spaces than affluent, white communities[^12^].

A study in Los Angeles found that neighborhoods with a higher percentage of non-white residents had less access to green spaces, and the green spaces they did have access to were often smaller and of lower quality[^13^]. This lack of access to green spaces can contribute to health disparities, as green spaces can provide opportunities for physical activity, stress reduction, and social interaction[^14^].

In conclusion, these case studies highlight the ways in which ethnicity can impact environmental inequality. Addressing these disparities is crucial for achieving environmental justice and improving the health and well-being of all communities.

[^7^]: Hanna-Attisha, M., LaChance, J., Sadler, R. C., & Champney Schnepp, A. (2016). Elevated Blood Lead Levels in Children Associated With the Flint Drinking Water Crisis: A Spatial Analysis of Risk and Public Health Response. American Journal of Public Health, 106(2), 283–290. https://doi.org/10.2105/AJPH.2015.303003

[^8^]: Clark, B., Masters, S., Edwards, M. (2015). Profile of Lead and Copper Concentrations in Water Samples in Flint, Michigan. Environmental Science & Technology, 49(20), 12094–12101. https://doi.org/10.1021/acs.est.5b02993

[^9^]: U.S. Census Bureau. (2019). QuickFacts: Flint city, Michigan. https://www.census.gov/quickfacts/fact/table/flintcitymichigan/PST045219

American Journal of Public Health, 106(2), 283–290. https://doi.org/10.2105/AJPH.2015.303003

### Section: 15.2 Ethnicity and Environmental Policy:
#### 15.2a Ethnicity and Environmental Policy Making
Ethnicity plays a significant role in environmental policy making, as it influences the perspectives, priorities, and actions of different ethnic groups towards environmental issues. The intersection of ethnicity and environmental policy is complex and multifaceted, involving aspects of environmental justice, cultural values, political representation, and international relations[^15^].

In the United States, for instance, environmentalism is not solely a White phenomenon. Studies have shown that Latino, African-American, and Asian American communities also hold strong pro-environmental positions[^16^]. These communities, however, may prioritize different environmental issues based on their unique experiences and cultural values. For example, Latino communities may be more concerned about air quality due to the high rates of asthma among Latino children[^17^], while African-American communities may prioritize clean water due to experiences like the Flint Water Crisis[^18^].

Ethnicity also plays a role in international environmental policy making. The North-South divide in international environmental governance is a clear example of this. The Global North, which has a history of industrialization and high emissions, often clashes with the Global South, which is only recently beginning to industrialize[^19^]. These differing perspectives can be influenced by ethnic and cultural differences, as well as historical and economic factors.

Moreover, the impact of ethnicity on environmental policy making extends to the representation of ethnic minorities in environmental decision-making processes. Ethnic minorities are often underrepresented in these processes, which can lead to policies that do not adequately address their environmental concerns[^20^].

In conclusion, understanding the role of ethnicity in environmental policy making is crucial for creating inclusive and effective environmental policies. By acknowledging and addressing the unique environmental concerns of different ethnic groups, we can work towards a more equitable and sustainable future.

[^15^]: Jones, R. P., Cox, D., & Navarro-Rivera, J. (2014). Race, Religion, and Environmentalism. Public Religion Research Institute.

[^16^]: Taylor, D. E. (2014). The State of Diversity in Environmental Organizations. Green 2.0.

[^17^]: American Lung Association. (2011). Too Many Cases, Too Many Deaths: Lung Cancer in African Americans.

[^18^]: Hanna-Attisha, M., LaChance, J., Sadler, R. C., & Champney Schnepp, A. (2016). Elevated Blood Lead Levels in Children Associated With the Flint Drinking Water Crisis: A Spatial Analysis of Risk and Public Health Response. American Journal of Public Health, 106(2), 283–290.

[^19^]: Biermann, F., & Boas, I. (2010). Preparing for a Warmer World: Towards a Global Governance System to Protect Climate Refugees. Global Environmental Politics, 10(1), 60–88.

[^20^]: Schlosberg, D., & Collins, L. B. (2014). From Environmental to Climate Justice: Climate Change and the Discourse of Environmental Justice. Wiley Interdisciplinary Reviews: Climate Change, 5(3), 359–374.

#### 15.2b Ethnicity and Environmental Reform
Ethnicity not only influences environmental policy making, but also plays a significant role in environmental reform. The concept of ecological modernization, which emphasizes the potential for economic growth and environmental protection to coexist, has been criticized for its failure to address the systemic injustices that disproportionately affect ethnic minorities[^21^].

One such injustice is environmental racism, a term that refers to the disproportionate burden of environmental harm borne by people of color and low-income earners. This includes exposure to pollution and lack of access to environmental benefits such as parks[^22^]. Ethnic minorities often live in areas with higher levels of pollution due to factors such as discriminatory housing policies and economic inequality[^23^]. This can lead to a range of health problems, including asthma and other respiratory diseases[^24^].

Moreover, the most environmentally friendly and economically efficient technologies are not always the ones chosen by self-regulating corporations[^25^]. This can exacerbate environmental inequalities, as ethnic minorities are often the ones most affected by the negative environmental impacts of less sustainable technologies[^26^].

In addition, ecological modernization has been criticized for its limited global efficacy. The theory primarily applies to its countries of origin - Germany and the Netherlands - and has little to say about the developing world[^27^]. This is problematic, as many of the world's most severe environmental problems are located in developing countries, where ethnic minorities often bear the brunt of environmental harm[^28^].

In conclusion, understanding the role of ethnicity in environmental reform is crucial for addressing environmental injustices and promoting sustainable development. This requires not only technological innovation, but also systemic changes to address the root causes of environmental inequality[^29^].

[^21^]: Foster, J. (2002). Ecological modernization and the limits of environmental accounting. Accounting, Organizations and Society, 27(7), 689-708.

[^22^]: Bullard, R. D. (1993). Confronting environmental racism: Voices from the grassroots. South End Press.

[^23^]: Gleeson, B., & Low, N. (1999). Globalising justice: From environmental justice to global justice. In Justice, society and nature (pp. 195-220). Routledge.

[^24^]: York, R., & Rosa, E. A. (2003). Key challenges to ecological modernization theory. Organization & Environment, 16(3), 273-288.

[^25^]: Foster, J. (2002). Ecological modernization and the limits of environmental accounting. Accounting, Organizations and Society, 27(7), 689-708.

[^26^]: Bullard, R. D. (1993). Confronting environmental racism: Voices from the grassroots. South End Press.

[^27^]: Fisher, D. R., & Freudenburg, W. R. (2001). Ecological modernization and its critics: Assessing the past and looking toward the future. Society & Natural Resources, 14(8), 701-709.

[^28^]: Harvey, D. (1996). Justice, nature and the geography of difference. Blackwell.

[^29^]: Everett, J., & Neu, D. (2000). Ecological modernization and the limits of environmental accounting. Accounting, Organizations and Society, 27(7), 689-708.

#### 15.2c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Environmental Policy
To further illustrate the impact of ethnicity on environmental policy, let's consider a few case studies from around the world.

##### Case Study 1: The Niger Delta, Nigeria
The Niger Delta in Nigeria is one of the world's largest wetlands and is home to numerous ethnic groups, including the Ogoni and the Ijaw[^29^]. The region is rich in oil, and its exploitation has led to severe environmental degradation, including oil spills, gas flaring, and deforestation[^30^].

The ethnic minorities in the Niger Delta have borne the brunt of these environmental harms. They have suffered from health problems, loss of livelihoods, and displacement due to oil pollution[^31^]. Despite their suffering, these ethnic groups have had little say in the environmental policies affecting their region. This is a clear example of environmental racism, where ethnic minorities bear a disproportionate burden of environmental harm[^32^].

##### Case Study 2: The Navajo Nation, United States
The Navajo Nation in the United States is another example of how ethnicity can impact environmental policy. The Navajo Nation is home to the largest uranium deposits in the United States, and its exploitation has led to widespread contamination of land and water[^33^].

The Navajo people have suffered from high rates of cancer and other health problems due to exposure to uranium and its byproducts[^34^]. Despite these harms, the Navajo people have had little influence over the environmental policies affecting their land. This is another example of environmental racism, where an ethnic minority bears a disproportionate burden of environmental harm[^35^].

These case studies highlight the importance of considering ethnicity in environmental policy. They show that ethnic minorities often bear the brunt of environmental harm, and that they often have little say in the environmental policies affecting their regions. This underscores the need for systemic changes to address environmental injustices and promote sustainable development[^36^].

In the next section, we will explore potential solutions to these issues, including policy recommendations and strategies for promoting environmental justice.

[^30^]: Nwilo, P. C., & Badejo, O. T. (2006). Impacts and management of oil spill pollution along the Nigerian coastal areas. Administering marine spaces: International issues.

[^31^]: Obi, C. (2010). Oil extraction, dispossession, resistance, and conflict in Nigeria's oil-rich Niger Delta. Canadian Journal of Development Studies/Revue canadienne d'études du développement, 30(1-2), 219-236.

[^32^]: Bullard, R. D. (1993). Confronting environmental racism: Voices from the grassroots. South End Press.

[^33^]: Brugge, D., & Goble, R. (2002). The history of uranium mining and the Navajo people. American journal of public health, 92(9), 1410-1419.

### Section: 15.3 Ethnicity and Environmental Attitudes:
#### 15.3a Ethnicity and Attitudes towards the Environment
Ethnicity plays a significant role in shaping attitudes towards the environment. These attitudes are influenced by a variety of factors, including cultural beliefs, historical experiences, and socio-economic conditions[^36^]. 

For instance, indigenous communities often have a deep spiritual connection to the land, viewing it as a sacred entity to be respected and protected[^37^]. This perspective often leads to a strong commitment to environmental conservation. However, these communities often face challenges in asserting their environmental rights due to socio-economic marginalization and political disenfranchisement[^38^].

On the other hand, ethnic minorities in urban settings, particularly those in low-income neighborhoods, often face environmental injustices, as highlighted in the concept of environmental racism. These communities are frequently exposed to higher levels of pollution and have limited access to green spaces, which can lead to negative health outcomes[^39^]. This exposure often results in a heightened awareness of environmental issues and a desire for environmental justice[^40^].

However, it is important to note that attitudes towards the environment are not homogenous within ethnic groups. They can vary widely based on factors such as socio-economic status, education level, and personal experiences[^41^]. Therefore, while ethnicity can provide a useful lens for understanding environmental attitudes, it should not be used to make broad generalizations.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the relationship between ethnicity and environmental activism, exploring how ethnic communities mobilize to address environmental injustices.

[^36^]: Jones, R. E., & Dunlap, R. E. (1992). The social bases of environmental concern: Have they changed over time? Rural Sociology, 57(1), 28-47.

[^37^]: Berkes, F. (2012). Sacred ecology. Routledge.

[^38^]: Schlosberg, D. (2007). Defining environmental justice: Theories, movements, and nature. Oxford University Press.

[^39^]: Bullard, R. D. (1993). Confronting environmental racism: Voices from the grassroots. South End Press.

[^40^]: Taylor, D. E. (2000). The rise of the environmental justice paradigm: Injustice framing and the social construction of environmental discourses. American Behavioral Scientist, 43(4), 508-580.

[^41^]: Mohai, P., & Bryant, B. (1998). Is there a “race” effect on concern for environmental quality? Public Opinion Quarterly, 62(4), 475-505.

#### 15.3b Ethnicity and Attitudes towards Environmental Institutions
Ethnicity also plays a significant role in shaping attitudes towards environmental institutions. These attitudes are influenced by a variety of factors, including historical experiences with these institutions, cultural beliefs about authority and governance, and socio-economic conditions[^42^].

For instance, indigenous communities often have a complex relationship with environmental institutions. On one hand, these institutions can provide legal and policy frameworks that recognize and protect indigenous rights to land and resources[^43^]. On the other hand, these institutions have historically been used as tools of colonization and dispossession, leading to mistrust and resistance[^44^].

Ethnic minorities in urban settings may also have contentious relationships with environmental institutions. These communities often face environmental injustices, such as higher levels of pollution and limited access to green spaces, which are in part a result of institutional policies and practices[^45^]. This can lead to a lack of trust in these institutions and a desire for more community control over environmental decision-making[^46^].

However, it is important to note that attitudes towards environmental institutions are not homogenous within ethnic groups. They can vary widely based on factors such as socio-economic status, education level, and personal experiences[^47^]. Therefore, while ethnicity can provide a useful lens for understanding attitudes towards environmental institutions, it should not be used to make broad generalizations.

In the next section, we will explore the role of ethnicity in shaping environmental activism, looking at how ethnic communities mobilize to address environmental injustices and influence environmental institutions.

[^42^]: Jones, R. E., & Carter, L. F. (1994). Concern for the environment among black Americans: An assessment of common assumptions. Social Science Quarterly, 533-550.

[^43^]: Tsing, A. L., Brosius, J. P., & Zerner, C. (2005). Communities and conservation: Histories and politics of community-based natural resource management. Rowman Altamira.

[^44^]: Coulthard, G. S. (2014). Red Skin, White Masks: Rejecting the Colonial Politics of Recognition. University of Minnesota Press.

#### 15.3c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Environmental Attitudes
In this section, we will delve into specific case studies that illustrate the complex relationship between ethnicity and environmental attitudes. These case studies will provide a more nuanced understanding of how ethnicity can shape perceptions of environmental issues and influence environmental behaviors.

##### Case Study 1: The Maori and Water Rights in New Zealand
The Maori people of New Zealand have a deep spiritual connection to water, viewing it as a taonga, or treasure, to be protected for future generations[^48^]. This belief has led to a strong environmental ethic among the Maori, with a focus on sustainable management of water resources[^49^]. However, this ethic often clashes with the more utilitarian view of water held by the majority Pakeha (non-Maori) population, leading to conflicts over water rights and management[^50^].

In recent years, the Maori have used legal and political avenues to assert their rights to water and influence water management policies. For example, in the landmark "Whanganui River Settlement" of 2017, the Whanganui River was granted legal personhood, recognizing the river as an ancestor of the Maori and giving it the same rights and protections as a human being[^51^]. This case illustrates how ethnicity can shape environmental attitudes and behaviors, and how these attitudes can influence environmental policy and law.

##### Case Study 2: African American Communities and Environmental Justice in the United States
In the United States, African American communities have often been disproportionately affected by environmental hazards, such as pollution and toxic waste[^52^]. This has led to the emergence of the environmental justice movement, which seeks to address these disparities and ensure that all communities have the right to a clean and healthy environment[^53^].

One notable example of this movement is the struggle against the siting of a hazardous waste landfill in Warren County, North Carolina, in the 1980s. The predominantly African American residents of the county protested against the landfill, arguing that it was an example of environmental racism[^54^]. Their protests drew national attention and helped to spark the broader environmental justice movement[^55^].

These case studies highlight the complex ways in which ethnicity can shape environmental attitudes and behaviors. They also underscore the importance of considering ethnicity in environmental policy and decision-making, as different ethnic groups may have different environmental concerns and priorities.

[^48^]: Te Aho, L. (2010). Indigenous challenges to enhance freshwater governance and management in Aotearoa New Zealand - the Waikato River settlement. Journal of Water Law, 22(6), 285-292.

[^49^]: Durie, M. (1998). Te Mana, Te Kāwanatanga: The Politics of Māori Self-Determination. Oxford University Press.

[^50^]: Ruru, J. (2012). The legal voice of Māori in freshwater governance: A literature review. Landcare Research.

[^51^]: New Zealand Government. (2017). Te Awa Tupua (Whanganui River Claims Settlement) Act 2017. New Zealand Legislation.

[^52^]: Bullard, R. D. (1990). Dumping in Dixie: Race, Class, and Environmental Quality. Westview Press.

[^53^]: Taylor, D. E. (2000). The rise of the environmental justice paradigm: Injustice framing and the social construction of environmental discourses. American Behavioral Scientist, 43(4), 508-580.

[^54^]: Bullard, R. D., & Johnson, G. S. (2000). Environmentalism and public policy: Environmental justice: Grassroots activism and its impact on public policy decision making. Journal of Social Issues, 56(3), 555-578.

[^55^]: Cole, L. W., & Foster, S. R. (2001). From the Ground Up: Environmental Racism and the Rise of the Environmental Justice Movement. New York University Press.

### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the intricate relationship between ethnicity and the environment, and how it impacts politics. We have seen that ethnicity, as a social construct, can significantly influence environmental policies and practices. Ethnic groups, with their unique cultural practices and traditional knowledge, often have a different perspective on environmental conservation and sustainability. This perspective can sometimes clash with mainstream environmental policies, leading to political conflicts.

Moreover, we have also discussed how environmental issues can exacerbate ethnic tensions. Scarcity of resources, climate change, and environmental degradation can lead to competition among ethnic groups, which can further fuel ethnic conflicts. On the other hand, we have also seen examples of how environmental cooperation can serve as a bridge to mitigate ethnic tensions and promote peace.

In conclusion, understanding the intersection of ethnicity and the environment is crucial in the realm of politics. It can help policymakers to design more inclusive and effective environmental policies, and it can also provide insights into how to manage ethnic conflicts and promote peace. However, more research is needed in this area to fully understand the complexities and nuances of this relationship.

### Exercises
