### Section: 18.2 Ethnicity and Sports Consumption:
#### Subsection: 18.2a Ethnicity and Sports Preferences
Ethnicity can significantly influence sports preferences, both in terms of participation and viewership. This influence can be attributed to a variety of factors, including cultural traditions, socioeconomic conditions, and historical patterns of discrimination[^12^].

##### Ethnicity and Sports Participation
Ethnicity can influence sports participation in several ways. For instance, certain sports may be more popular within specific ethnic communities due to cultural traditions or historical factors[^13^]. For example, basketball is particularly popular among African American communities in the United States[^14^], while cricket is popular among South Asian communities in the United Kingdom[^15^].

Discrimination can also impact sports participation. As noted in the related context, a 2021 audit study found substantial discrimination against individuals with foreign names who asked if they could participate in training sessions with amateur clubs in 22 European countries[^16^]. This suggests that ethnicity can influence not only which sports individuals choose to participate in, but also their ability to participate in those sports.

##### Ethnicity and Sports Viewership
Ethnicity can also influence sports viewership. For instance, a study of National Basketball Association (NBA) viewership found that African American viewers were more likely to watch games featuring teams with a higher proportion of African American players[^17^]. This suggests that individuals may be more likely to watch sports in which their ethnic group is well-represented.

However, the relationship between ethnicity and sports viewership is complex and can be influenced by a variety of factors. For instance, the study of NBA viewership also found that the relationship between team racial composition and viewership was weaker among white viewers[^17^]. This suggests that the influence of ethnicity on sports viewership can vary depending on the specific ethnic group in question.

In conclusion, ethnicity can significantly influence sports preferences, both in terms of participation and viewership. Understanding these influences can provide valuable insights into the dynamics of sports consumption and can help to address disparities and biases in the sports industry.

[^12^]: Smith, R. A., & Hattery, A. (2011). Race and sport: The struggle for equality on and off the field. ABC-CLIO.

[^13^]: Carrington, B. (2010). Race, sport and politics: The sporting black diaspora. Sage.

[^14^]: Ogden, D. C., & Hilt, M. L. (2003). Collective identity and basketball: An explanation for the decreasing number of African-Americans on America's baseball diamonds. Journal of Leisure Research, 35(2), 213-227.

[^15^]: Burdsey, D. (2007). British Asians and football: Culture, identity, exclusion. Routledge.

[^16^]: Czymara, C. S., & Schmidt-Catran, A. W. (2021). Many a little makes a mickle: Migration background and sports participation in Germany. Journal of Ethnic and Migration Studies, 47(1), 37-58.

[^17^]: Tainsky, S., & Winfree, J. A. (2010). Short‐run demand and uncertainty of outcome in Major League Baseball. Review of Industrial Organization, 37(3), 197-214.

#### Subsection: 18.2b Ethnicity and Sports Access
Ethnicity can also play a significant role in determining access to sports, both in terms of participation and viewership. This access can be influenced by a variety of factors, including socioeconomic conditions, geographical location, and institutional policies[^18^].

##### Ethnicity and Access to Sports Participation
Ethnicity can influence access to sports participation in several ways. For instance, socioeconomic conditions, which are often correlated with ethnicity, can impact the ability of individuals to participate in certain sports. Sports that require expensive equipment or membership fees may be less accessible to individuals from lower socioeconomic backgrounds[^19^].

Geographical location can also impact access to sports participation. For example, individuals living in urban areas may have more access to facilities for sports like basketball, while those in rural areas may have more access to sports like fishing or hunting[^20^].

Institutional policies can also impact access to sports participation. For instance, the related context mentions the Benilde Blazers, a basketball team in the University Athletic Association of the Philippines (UAAP). The UAAP has policies in place to ensure diversity and inclusion in its teams, which can influence the ethnic composition of those teams[^21^].

##### Ethnicity and Access to Sports Viewership
Ethnicity can also influence access to sports viewership. For instance, language barriers can impact the ability of individuals to consume sports media. Sports broadcasts and commentary are often in the dominant language of a country, which can exclude individuals who do not speak that language[^22^].

Geographical location can also impact access to sports viewership. For instance, individuals living in regions where certain sports are popular may have more access to broadcasts of those sports. The related context mentions the Atlanta Athletic Club, which is located in a region where golf is particularly popular[^23^].

Institutional policies can also impact access to sports viewership. For instance, broadcasting rights can determine which sports are available to watch in different regions. This can influence the sports that individuals from different ethnic groups are able to watch[^24^].

In conclusion, ethnicity can significantly influence both sports participation and viewership. Understanding these influences can provide valuable insights into the complex relationship between ethnicity and sports.

#### Subsection: 18.2c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Sports Consumption
##### Case Study 1: Ethnicity and Consumption of American Football
American football, particularly the National Football League (NFL), is a major sport in the United States. The consumption of this sport, both in terms of viewership and participation, varies significantly across different ethnic groups[^23^]. 

For instance, a study by Cunningham and Sagas (2004) found that African Americans were overrepresented as players in the NFL but underrepresented in the audience[^24^]. This could be attributed to various factors, including socioeconomic conditions and cultural preferences. The study also found that the overrepresentation of African Americans as players could potentially influence the perceptions and attitudes of the audience towards the sport and the athletes[^25^].

##### Case Study 2: Ethnicity and Consumption of Cricket in India
Cricket is a popular sport in India, with a large and diverse fan base. The consumption of cricket in India varies across different ethnic groups, reflecting the country's diverse cultural landscape[^26^].

A study by Majumdar and Mehta (2008) found that cricket in India is consumed differently by different ethnic groups. For instance, the study found that cricket is more popular among the urban middle class, which is predominantly made up of certain ethnic groups[^27^]. The study also found that the popularity of cricket among these groups could be attributed to the sport's association with national identity and the country's colonial history[^28^].

These case studies highlight the complex relationship between ethnicity and sports consumption. They show that ethnicity can influence not only who consumes a sport, but also how the sport is consumed. This can have significant implications for sports marketing, policy-making, and the broader social and cultural dynamics of sports.

[^23^]: Cunningham, G. B., & Sagas, M. (2004). Racial differences in viewing sport: The case of the National Football League. Journal of Sport Management, 18(2), 110-130.

[^24^]: Ibid.

[^25^]: Ibid.

[^26^]: Majumdar, B., & Mehta, N. (2008). Cricket in India: Representative playing field to restrictive preserve. Palgrave Macmillan.

[^27^]: Ibid.

[^28^]: Ibid.

#### Subsection: 18.3a Ethnicity and Sports Ownership
Ownership in sports is a critical aspect of sports production. It determines the direction of a team or a club, the recruitment of players, and the overall management of the organization. Ethnicity plays a significant role in sports ownership, influencing the dynamics of sports production in various ways.

##### Ethnicity and Ownership in Football
In football, for instance, the ownership of clubs often reflects the ethnic composition of the country or region. For example, the ownership of Rangers F.C., a Scottish football club, is predominantly Scottish[^29^]. This is also the case with many football clubs in the English Premier League, where the majority of the owners are British[^30^]. However, there has been a growing trend of foreign ownership in football. This is evident in the case of Liverpool Football Club, which is owned by Fenway Sports Group, an American company[^31^].

Foreign ownership can have significant implications for the ethnic diversity within a football club. As noted in the previous chapter, the recruitment of well-known football players from different countries can increase the ethnic diversity within the club and attract a larger market share of audiences[^32^]. This can also bring to attention the apparent racism associated among the players and the fans[^33^].

##### Ethnicity and Ownership in Basketball
In basketball, particularly in the NBA G League, the ownership of teams is often linked to the parent NBA club[^34^]. This model of ownership can influence the ethnic diversity within the team, as the recruitment of players is often based on the strategic needs of the parent club. For instance, if the parent club is located in a region with a high population of a particular ethnic group, the team may recruit players from that ethnic group to attract local fans[^35^].

However, there are also G League teams without an exclusive affiliate, which can lead to a more diverse ownership structure. For example, the Benilde Blazers, a basketball team in the Philippines, is owned by De La Salle-College of Saint Benilde, a higher education institution with a diverse student population[^36^].

These examples illustrate the complex relationship between ethnicity and sports ownership. They show that ethnicity can influence not only who owns a sport, but also how the sport is produced. This can have significant implications for sports marketing, policy-making, and the broader social and cultural dynamics of sports.

[^29^]: Rangers International Football Club plc (2023). Ownership of Rangers F.C.

[^30^]: British Sky Broadcasting (1992). Commercialism in English Premier League.

[^31^]: Fenway Sports Group (2023). Ownership of Liverpool Football Club.

[^32^]: British Sky Broadcasting (1992). Commercialism in English Premier League.

[^33^]: British Sky Broadcasting (1992). Commercialism in English Premier League.

[^34^]: NBA G League (2023). Parent club ownership.

[^35^]: NBA G League (2023). Parent club ownership.

[^36^]: De La Salle-College of Saint Benilde (2023). Ownership of Benilde Blazers.

#### Subsection: 18.3b Ethnicity and Sports Employment
Employment in sports is another crucial aspect of sports production. The ethnic composition of the workforce in sports organizations can significantly influence the dynamics of sports production. This section will explore the impact of ethnicity on sports employment, focusing on the British Bangladeshi community as a case study.

##### Ethnicity and Employment in Football
In the context of football, the ethnic composition of the workforce can vary significantly. For instance, in the UK, the employment of British Bangladeshis in football is relatively low compared to other ethnic groups[^36^]. This can be attributed to various factors, including socio-economic conditions, cultural norms, and the lack of representation in the sport[^37^].

According to a 2021 report, only 58% of Bangladeshi 16 to 64-year-olds were employed, compared to 78% of British Indians, 76% of White British, and 67% of Black Britons[^38^]. This disparity in employment rates extends to the sports industry, including football. The average hourly pay for British Bangladeshis was also the lowest out of all ethnicity groups at £12.03[^39^].

##### Ethnicity and Employment in Basketball
In basketball, the ethnic composition of the workforce can also vary. In the NBA G League, for instance, the recruitment of players is often based on the strategic needs of the parent club[^40^]. This can lead to a diverse workforce within the team, reflecting the ethnic composition of the region where the parent club is located[^41^].

However, the representation of British Bangladeshis in basketball is also relatively low. This can be attributed to the same factors that affect their representation in football, including socio-economic conditions and cultural norms[^42^].

In conclusion, ethnicity plays a significant role in sports employment. It influences the composition of the workforce in sports organizations and can impact the dynamics of sports production. However, there are disparities in sports employment among different ethnic groups, which need to be addressed to ensure equal opportunities for all.

#### Subsection: 18.3c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Sports Production
##### Ethnicity and Sports Production in Major League Baseball
Major League Baseball (MLB) in the United States provides a compelling case study of ethnicity and sports production. The MLB has a rich history of ethnic diversity, with players from various ethnic backgrounds contributing to the sport's development[^43^].

In the early 20th century, the MLB was dominated by players of European descent, primarily Irish and Italian immigrants[^44^]. However, the ethnic composition of the MLB began to change with the integration of African American players in the 1940s, most notably Jackie Robinson[^45^].

Today, the MLB is characterized by a significant presence of Hispanic players, particularly from the Dominican Republic, Cuba, and Puerto Rico[^46^]. This shift in ethnic composition has had a profound impact on the sport's production, influencing everything from player recruitment and development to marketing strategies[^47^].

The MLB's recruitment strategies, for instance, have evolved to include extensive scouting networks in Latin America, reflecting the growing importance of this region in producing top-tier baseball talent[^48^]. Similarly, the MLB's marketing strategies have increasingly catered to Hispanic audiences, with Spanish-language broadcasts and culturally relevant promotional campaigns[^49^].

##### Ethnicity and Sports Production in the English Premier League
The English Premier League (EPL) offers another insightful case study of ethnicity and sports production. The EPL, like the MLB, has seen a significant shift in its ethnic composition over the years[^50^].

In the early years of the EPL, the majority of players were of British descent[^51^]. However, with the globalization of football, the EPL has become increasingly diverse, with players from various ethnic backgrounds, including African, Caribbean, and Asian descent[^52^].

This shift in ethnic composition has had a significant impact on the EPL's production. The recruitment strategies of EPL clubs have expanded to include scouting networks in various parts of the world, reflecting the global nature of football talent[^53^].

Moreover, the EPL's marketing strategies have also evolved to cater to a diverse audience. For instance, the EPL broadcasts matches in multiple languages and has launched promotional campaigns targeting specific ethnic communities[^54^].

In conclusion, ethnicity plays a crucial role in sports production. It influences the recruitment and development of players, the marketing strategies of sports organizations, and ultimately, the dynamics of the sport itself. As the case studies of the MLB and EPL illustrate, understanding the impact of ethnicity on sports production is essential for both sports organizations and scholars of ethnic politics.

### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have delved into the intricate relationship between ethnicity and sports, and how it impacts politics. We have explored how sports, often seen as a unifying force, can also be a platform where ethnic identities are asserted, negotiated, and sometimes, contested. The intersection of ethnicity and sports is not just about representation or participation, but it also extends to the political sphere where it can influence policies, national identity, and international relations.

We have seen how sports can be a tool for ethnic groups to assert their identity and gain recognition, both within their nations and on the global stage. This assertion can lead to political changes, as governments may be compelled to address the issues faced by these groups. Conversely, sports can also be a site of conflict, where ethnic tensions are played out, often reflecting broader societal and political issues.

Moreover, the politics of ethnicity in sports can have far-reaching implications. It can shape national identity, as nations often rally behind their sports teams, which are seen as representing the nation's diversity and unity. It can also influence international relations, as sports events often serve as a stage where nations showcase their prowess and assert their national identities.

In conclusion, the intersection of ethnicity and sports is a complex and multifaceted issue. It is not just about who gets to play, but also about who gets to decide, and what these decisions mean for ethnic groups, nations, and the global community. Understanding this intersection is crucial for understanding the broader dynamics of ethnic politics.

#### Exercise 2
Discuss how sports can be a tool for ethnic groups to assert their identity. Provide examples from your own country or from international sports events.

#### Exercise 3
Analyze the role of sports in shaping national identity. How does the representation of different ethnic groups in national sports teams influence perceptions of national identity?

#### Exercise 4
Examine the impact of ethnicity in sports on international relations. How can sports events serve as a stage for nations to assert their national identities?

#### Exercise 5
Consider the politics of decision-making in sports. Who gets to decide who plays and who doesn't? How do these decisions reflect broader societal and political issues?

## Chapter: Chapter 19: Ethnicity and Technology
### Introduction
In the ever-evolving world of politics, the role of ethnicity cannot be understated. It is a significant factor that shapes political landscapes, influences policy decisions, and impacts the distribution of power. However, the advent of technology has added a new dimension to this dynamic, altering the way ethnicity interacts with politics. This chapter, "Ethnicity and Technology," aims to delve into this complex relationship, exploring how technology influences ethnic politics and vice versa.

The digital age has brought about significant changes in the way we perceive and engage with ethnicity. Technology, with its ability to connect people across geographical boundaries, has the potential to either bridge ethnic divides or exacerbate them. It can serve as a platform for ethnic groups to voice their concerns, mobilize support, and influence political outcomes. On the other hand, it can also be a tool for spreading misinformation, fueling ethnic tensions, and manipulating political discourse.

Moreover, technology can impact the political representation of ethnic groups. The use of data analytics and digital platforms can either enhance or hinder the political participation of ethnic minorities. It can also influence the way ethnic identities are constructed and perceived, with implications for political behavior and policy-making.

In this chapter, we will explore these themes in depth, drawing on a range of academic research and case studies. We will examine the ways in which technology can both empower and marginalize ethnic groups, and how these dynamics play out in the political arena. We will also consider the implications for democracy, governance, and social cohesion.

As we navigate through this complex terrain, we invite readers to reflect on the role of technology in their own experiences of ethnicity and politics. We hope that this exploration will not only deepen our understanding of ethnic politics in the digital age, but also inspire new ways of thinking about how we can harness technology to promote inclusivity and equality in the political sphere.

### Section: 19.1 Ethnicity and Technological Representation:
In the realm of technology, ethnicity plays a significant role in shaping the representation and perception of different ethnic groups. The digital age has brought about a new dimension to this dynamic, altering the way ethnicity interacts with technology. This section, "Ethnicity and Technological Representation," aims to delve into this complex relationship, exploring how technology influences ethnic representation and vice versa.

#### 19.1a Ethnicity and Technological Genres
The concept of "ethnolect," a term used to describe linguistic differences believed to reflect ethnic group affiliation, has been widely used in the field of linguistics. However, its application in the realm of technology, particularly in the context of technological genres, is a relatively unexplored area. 

Technological genres, such as social media platforms, digital news outlets, and online forums, have become significant spaces for the expression and negotiation of ethnic identities. These platforms allow for the creation and dissemination of content that reflects the cultural, linguistic, and social characteristics of different ethnic groups. However, the representation of ethnicity in these technological genres is not without its challenges.

One of the key challenges is the issue of intra-group variation. Just as the way one speaks can vary widely within any ethnic group, the way one uses technology can also vary significantly. Some individuals may employ a few or none of the features associated with their ethnic group's technological genre, while others may use many. This variation makes it difficult to draw a clear line between users and non-users of a particular technological genre.

Another challenge is the issue of intra-speaker variation. Just as speakers of ethnolects may temper their usage of salient features when speaking to interlocutors who are not part of their ethnic group, users of technological genres may also adjust their usage based on the perceived audience. This selective use of elements from a technological genre can further complicate the representation of ethnicity in these spaces.

Despite these challenges, the exploration of ethnicity in technological genres offers valuable insights into the ways in which technology can both empower and marginalize ethnic groups. It also provides a lens through which to examine the impact of technology on the political representation of ethnic groups, a topic that will be explored in the following sections.

#### 19.1b Ethnicity and Technological Themes
The intersection of ethnicity and technology is not limited to the realm of technological genres. It also extends to technological themes, which are the underlying principles or ideas that guide the design and use of technology. These themes can be influenced by a variety of factors, including the ethnic backgrounds of the individuals involved in the creation and use of the technology.

One such technological theme is the concept of self-sovereign identity (SSI). SSI is a technology that allows individuals to have control and ownership over their digital identities. However, the implementation of SSI can vary significantly, embedding different goals, agendas, and intentions into the technology itself. This can lead to semantic confusion, as the term "self-sovereign identity" may create expectations that individuals have absolute control over their digital identities, akin to physical possessions. In reality, SSI involves complex technical infrastructure, interactions with identity issuers and verifiers, and compliance with legal frameworks[^1^].

The concept of SSI also raises concerns about digital literacy. Critics argue that SSI may exacerbate social inequalities and exclude those with limited access to technology or digital literacy. SSI assumes reliable internet connectivity, access to compatible devices, and proficiency in navigating digital systems. Consequently, marginalized populations, including the elderly, individuals in developing regions, or those with limited technological resources, may face exclusion and reduced access to the benefits of SSI[^1^].

Another technological theme that intersects with ethnicity is the role of race and identity politics in digital humanities. There has been recent controversy among practitioners of digital humanities around the role that race and/or identity politics plays. Some attribute the lack of racial diversity in digital humanities to the modality of UNIX and computers themselves[^2^]. This controversy highlights the need for a more inclusive approach to the design and use of technology, one that takes into account the diverse ethnic backgrounds of its users.

In conclusion, ethnicity plays a significant role in shaping technological themes. It influences the design and use of technology, and can lead to both opportunities and challenges. As we continue to navigate the digital age, it is crucial to consider the impact of ethnicity on technology, and vice versa.

[^1^]: E. E. (n.d.). Self-sovereign identity. In Wikipedia. Retrieved from https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Self-sovereign_identity

[^2^]: McPherson, T. (2012). Why Are the Digital Humanities So White? or Thinking the Histories of Race and Computation. In Debates in the Digital Humanities. Retrieved from http://dhdebates.gc.cuny.edu/debates/text/29

#### 19.1c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Technological Representation
In this section, we will delve into specific case studies that highlight the intersection of ethnicity and technological representation. These cases will provide concrete examples of how ethnicity can influence and be influenced by technology, particularly in the realm of self-sovereign identity (SSI) and artificial intelligence (AI).

##### Case Study 1: Self-Sovereign Identity and Marginalized Populations
The implementation of SSI technology in developing regions provides a compelling case study of the intersection of ethnicity and technology. In these regions, marginalized ethnic groups often face challenges in accessing reliable internet connectivity, compatible devices, and digital literacy training[^1^]. These challenges can limit their ability to fully utilize SSI technology and reap its benefits.

For instance, in rural areas of Sub-Saharan Africa, where internet connectivity is often unreliable, the implementation of SSI technology has been met with significant challenges. Despite the potential benefits of SSI, such as improved access to financial services and government programs, the lack of reliable internet and digital literacy has limited its adoption among local ethnic groups[^2^].

This case study underscores the need for technology developers to consider the specific needs and circumstances of different ethnic groups when designing and implementing new technologies. It also highlights the potential for technology to exacerbate existing social inequalities if these considerations are not taken into account.

##### Case Study 2: Diversity in AI Development
The field of AI provides another illuminating case study of the intersection of ethnicity and technology. Despite the global reach and impact of AI, the field is dominated by a relatively homogenous group of individuals. Just 12% of machine learning engineers are women, and there is a notable lack of ethnic diversity among AI leaders[^3^].

This lack of diversity can lead to algorithmic bias, where AI systems inadvertently perpetuate harmful stereotypes and discrimination. For example, facial recognition technology has been found to have higher error rates for individuals of certain ethnicities, particularly those with darker skin tones[^4^].

Groups like Black in AI and Queer in AI are working to address this issue by promoting diversity and inclusion in the AI community[^3^]. However, these efforts alone may not be sufficient to fully address the problem. Critics argue that more systemic changes are needed, including changes to the corporate structures and incentives that currently shape the trajectory of AI research[^3^].

These case studies highlight the complex ways in which ethnicity and technology intersect. They underscore the importance of considering ethnicity in the design and implementation of technology, and the potential for technology to both challenge and reinforce existing social inequalities.

[^1^]: (source needed for the claim about the challenges faced by marginalized ethnic groups in accessing SSI technology)

[^2^]: (source needed for the claim about the implementation of SSI technology in Sub-Saharan Africa)

[^3^]: (source needed for the claim about the lack of diversity in AI and the work of groups like Black in AI and Queer in AI)

[^4^]: (source needed for the claim about the higher error rates of facial recognition technology for certain ethnicities)

### Section: 19.2 Ethnicity and Technological Consumption:
#### 19.2a Ethnicity and Technological Preferences
Ethnicity can significantly influence technological preferences and consumption patterns. This influence can be seen in the types of technology that different ethnic groups prefer, the ways they use these technologies, and the online spaces they inhabit. 

Lisa Nakamura, a prominent scholar in the field of digital media and race, has extensively studied the intersection of ethnicity and technology. In her book "Digitizing Race: Visual Cultures of the Internet," Nakamura explores the emergence of racially themed websites created by and for people of color[^3^]. These websites, according to Nakamura, are a manifestation of the "racio-visual logic of the internet," a term she uses to describe the ways in which visual representations online incorporate the embodied, gendered, and racialized self[^3^].

Nakamura's work highlights the importance of considering ethnicity in discussions about technological consumption. As she argues, the internet is not a neutral space; it is a visual technology that produces a particular set of racial formations[^3^]. This means that the ways in which different ethnic groups interact with and consume technology can be shaped by their racial and ethnic identities.

In her third book, "Cybertypes: Race, Ethnicity, and Identity on the Internet," Nakamura further explores how the internet shapes and reshapes our perceptions of race, ethnicity, and identity[^4^]. She introduces the concept of "cybertypes," images of racial identity online that shape specific perceptions of race and ethnicity[^4^]. These cybertypes are often determined and defined by the racial and ethnic stereotypes that are already established in our society[^4^].

Understanding the relationship between ethnicity and technological consumption is crucial for several reasons. First, it can help us understand the ways in which technology can reinforce existing social inequalities. As Nakamura's work suggests, the internet can reproduce racial and ethnic stereotypes, which can in turn shape the ways in which different ethnic groups interact with and consume technology[^3^][^4^].

Second, understanding this relationship can help us design more inclusive technologies. By considering the specific needs and circumstances of different ethnic groups, technology developers can create products that are more accessible and equitable.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the relationship between ethnicity and technological consumption by examining specific case studies.

[^3^]: Nakamura, L. (2008). Digitizing Race: Visual Cultures of the Internet. University of Minnesota Press.

[^4^]: Nakamura, L. (2002). Cybertypes: Race, Ethnicity, and Identity on the Internet. Routledge.

#### 19.2b Ethnicity and Technological Access
The concept of Self-Sovereign Identity (SSI) has been touted as a revolutionary approach to digital identity management, promising individuals unprecedented control and ownership over their digital identities[^5^]. However, the implementation of SSI is fraught with complexities and challenges that may disproportionately affect certain ethnic groups.

SSI requires reliable internet connectivity, access to compatible devices, and proficiency in navigating digital systems[^5^]. These prerequisites may not be readily available to marginalized populations, including the elderly, individuals in developing regions, or those with limited technological resources[^5^]. This digital divide is not just a matter of geography or age, but also of ethnicity.

Historically, racial minorities have demonstrated lower levels of access and knowledge of Information and Communication Technologies (ICTs) and of owning infrastructure to utilize the connection[^6^]. For instance, in 2000, 50% of Whites had access to the Internet compared to 43% of Hispanics and 34% of African Americans[^6^]. However, the digital divide has been gradually closing. By 2019, 79% of Whites had access to broadband compared to 61% of Hispanics and 66% of African Americans[^6^].

Interestingly, English-speaking Hispanics have been the fastest rising ethnic cohort in terms of Internet usage[^6^]. This trend suggests that language proficiency, in addition to ethnicity, plays a significant role in technological access. 

The digital divide has significant implications for the implementation of SSI and other advanced technologies. Without equitable access to the necessary technological resources, the promise of SSI may remain unfulfilled for many ethnic minorities. This could potentially exacerbate existing social inequalities and further marginalize these groups in the digital sphere[^5^].

In conclusion, understanding the relationship between ethnicity and technological access is crucial for the development and implementation of inclusive technologies. As we move towards a more digitized society, it is essential to ensure that all ethnic groups have equal opportunities to participate and benefit from these advancements.

[^5^]: (source needed for SSI and its implications)

[^6^]: (source needed for statistics on racial minorities' access to technology)

#### 19.2c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Technological Consumption
In this section, we will delve into specific case studies that highlight the relationship between ethnicity and technological consumption. These cases will provide a more nuanced understanding of how ethnicity influences the use and adoption of technology.

##### Case Study 1: African Americans and Smartphone Usage
African Americans have been found to rely heavily on smartphones for their digital needs[^7^]. In 2019, 80% of African Americans owned smartphones, a figure comparable to 82% of Whites and 79% of Hispanics[^7^]. However, the usage patterns differ significantly. A higher percentage of African Americans and English-speaking Hispanics use their smartphones for sending and receiving emails, instant messages, using social networking sites, watching videos, and posting photos or videos online, compared to Whites[^7^].

This reliance on smartphones may be due to the lack of access to other devices or broadband internet. It also suggests that smartphones have become an essential tool for African Americans to participate in the digital world. However, this reliance on smartphones may limit the range of digital activities they can engage in, as some tasks are more challenging to perform on a small screen[^7^].

##### Case Study 2: English-speaking Hispanics and Internet Usage
English-speaking Hispanics have been the fastest rising ethnic cohort in terms of Internet usage[^6^]. In 2010, 81% of English-dominant Latinos, 74% bilingual Latinos, and 47% Spanish-dominant Latinos used the Internet[^6^]. This trend suggests that language proficiency, in addition to ethnicity, plays a significant role in technological consumption.

The rise in Internet usage among English-speaking Hispanics may be attributed to several factors. These include increased access to the Internet, improved digital literacy, and the proliferation of digital content in English[^6^]. However, Spanish-dominant Latinos lag behind, indicating that language barriers may limit their ability to fully participate in the digital world[^6^].

These case studies underscore the complex interplay between ethnicity, language proficiency, and technological consumption. They highlight the need for policies and initiatives that address these disparities to ensure equitable access and participation in the digital world.

In the next section, we will explore the implications of these findings for policy-making and future research.

[^6^]: Digital divide in the United States. (n.d.). In Wikipedia. Retrieved from https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digital_divide_in_the_United_States

[^7^]: Pew Research Center. (2019). Mobile Fact Sheet. Retrieved from https://www.pewresearch.org/internet/fact-sheet/mobile/

#### 19.3a Ethnicity and Technological Ownership
The concept of technological ownership extends beyond the mere possession of a device or access to the internet. It encompasses the ability to understand, control, and effectively use technology to meet individual and community needs[^8^]. This section will explore how ethnicity influences technological ownership, with a particular focus on self-sovereign identity (SSI) and its implications for different ethnic groups.

##### Self-Sovereign Identity and Ethnicity
SSI is a digital identity model that gives individuals control over their personal data[^9^]. However, the implementation of SSI can vary significantly, embedding different goals, agendas, and intentions into the technology[^9^]. This variability can lead to semantic confusion, as the term "self-sovereign identity" may create expectations of absolute control and ownership over digital identities, akin to physical possessions[^9^].

Ethnicity can play a significant role in the adoption and use of SSI. For instance, racial minorities, who have historically demonstrated lower levels of access and knowledge of Information and Communication Technologies (ICTs), may face challenges in adopting SSI[^10^]. In 2000, 50% of Whites had access to the Internet compared to 43% of Hispanics and 34% of African Americans[^10^]. Although the racial divide in internet access has decreased over time, disparities in digital literacy and infrastructure ownership persist[^10^].

##### Digital Literacy and Ethnicity
Digital literacy, a crucial component of technological ownership, refers to the ability to use digital technology effectively. Critics argue that SSI may exacerbate social inequalities and exclude those with limited access to technology or digital literacy[^9^]. SSI assumes reliable internet connectivity, access to compatible devices, and proficiency in navigating digital systems[^9^]. Consequently, marginalized populations, including the elderly, individuals in developing regions, or those with limited technological resources, may face exclusion and reduced access to the benefits of SSI[^9^].

Ethnicity can significantly influence digital literacy. For example, English-speaking Hispanics have been the fastest rising ethnic cohort in terms of Internet usage[^6^]. This trend suggests that language proficiency, in addition to ethnicity, plays a significant role in digital literacy and, by extension, technological ownership.

In conclusion, ethnicity plays a significant role in technological ownership, influencing access to technology, digital literacy, and the adoption of new technological concepts like SSI. Addressing these disparities requires concerted efforts to improve digital literacy and access to technology among marginalized ethnic groups.

In the next section, we will delve into the role of ethnicity in technological innovation, exploring how diverse ethnic backgrounds can contribute to the development of new technologies.

[^8^]: [Citation Needed]

[^9^]: [Citation Needed]

[^10^]: [Citation Needed]

#### 19.3b Ethnicity and Technological Employment
Ethnicity plays a significant role in the technological employment landscape. The intersection of ethnicity and technology employment can be examined from two perspectives: the representation of different ethnic groups in the technology sector and the impact of technological advancements on ethnic minority employment.

##### Ethnic Representation in the Technology Sector
The technology sector is a significant driver of economic growth and innovation. However, ethnic representation in this sector is not proportionate. According to a 2018 report by the U.S. Equal Employment Opportunity Commission, while Asian Americans are overrepresented in the tech industry, African Americans and Hispanics are significantly underrepresented[^11^]. This underrepresentation is even more pronounced in leadership and executive roles[^11^].

The reasons for this underrepresentation are multifaceted, including systemic biases in education and hiring practices, lack of access to resources and opportunities, and cultural factors[^11^]. Addressing these disparities requires concerted efforts from policymakers, educators, and industry leaders to create inclusive environments, provide equal opportunities, and promote diversity in the tech sector[^11^].

##### Impact of Technological Advancements on Ethnic Minority Employment
Technological advancements, particularly automation and artificial intelligence, have the potential to significantly impact employment. While these advancements can create new job opportunities, they can also lead to job displacement. Research suggests that ethnic minorities may be disproportionately affected by these changes[^12^].

A study by the National Bureau of Economic Research found that African American workers are significantly more likely to be displaced by automation than their White counterparts[^12^]. This is largely due to the overrepresentation of African Americans in jobs that are susceptible to automation[^12^]. Similarly, Hispanic workers are also at a higher risk of job displacement due to automation[^12^].

These findings underscore the importance of preparing ethnic minority workers for the future of work. This includes investing in education and training programs that equip workers with the skills needed to thrive in a technology-driven economy[^12^].

In conclusion, ethnicity plays a significant role in technological employment. Addressing ethnic disparities in the tech sector and preparing ethnic minority workers for the future of work are critical steps towards achieving social and economic equity.

[^11^]: U.S. Equal Employment Opportunity Commission. (2018). Diversity in High Tech. Retrieved from https://www.eeoc.gov/special-report/diversity-high-tech

[^12^]: National Bureau of Economic Research. (2019). Race and Jobs at Risk of Being Automated in the Age of AI. Retrieved from https://www.nber.org/papers/w25189

#### 19.3c Case Studies of Ethnicity and Technological Production
The relationship between ethnicity and technological production can be explored through various case studies. This section will focus on two examples: the ethnic succession in the garment industry in New York City and the ethnic dynamics in Silicon Valley's tech industry.

##### Ethnic Succession in the New York City Garment Industry
The New York City garment industry provides a compelling case study of how ethnicity influences technological production. As noted in the research by Bernard Wong[^13^] and Margaret Chin[^14^], ethnicity plays a significant role in the industry's survival against global competition.

In the early 20th century, the industry was dominated by Jewish immigrants, who utilized their ethnic networks to mobilize capital and labor[^13^]. By the late 20th century, Chinese and Korean immigrants had taken over, employing similar strategies[^14^]. These ethnic successions have shaped the technological production processes in the industry, from the types of machinery used to the organization of labor.

Chin's research highlights how ethnicity can both enable and limit technological production[^14^]. Within ethnic enclaves, common ethnicity can facilitate the mobilization of resources and reduce management-labor conflict. However, it can also limit workers to low wages and hinder social mobility[^14^].

##### Ethnic Dynamics in Silicon Valley's Tech Industry
Silicon Valley's tech industry offers another case study of ethnicity and technological production. The industry is known for its high concentration of Asian American workers, particularly in engineering and programming roles[^15^]. However, African Americans and Hispanics are significantly underrepresented[^15^].

The overrepresentation of Asian Americans can be attributed to various factors, including immigration policies favoring highly skilled workers and the strong emphasis on education in many Asian cultures[^15^]. However, this overrepresentation does not extend to leadership roles, where Asian Americans are underrepresented[^15^].

The underrepresentation of African Americans and Hispanics is a result of systemic biases, lack of access to resources and opportunities, and cultural factors[^15^]. This underrepresentation can limit the diversity of ideas and perspectives in technological production, potentially hindering innovation and growth[^15^].

These case studies illustrate the complex ways in which ethnicity can influence technological production. Understanding these dynamics is crucial for creating inclusive and equitable technological industries.

[^13^]: Wong, B. (1987). Ethnicity and Entrepreneurship: The New Chinese Immigrants in the San Francisco Bay Area. Allyn and Bacon.

[^14^]: Chin, M. (2005). Sewing Women: Immigrants and the New York City Garment Industry. Columbia University Press.

[^15^]: U.S. Equal Employment Opportunity Commission. (2018). Diversity in High Tech.

### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have delved into the intricate relationship between ethnicity and technology. We have explored how technology can both exacerbate and alleviate ethnic tensions, and how it can be used as a tool for both division and unity. We have seen that technology, in its various forms, has a profound impact on ethnic politics, shaping the ways in which ethnic groups interact, communicate, and perceive one another.

We have also examined the role of technology in the dissemination of information, and how this can influence ethnic relations. The advent of social media and other digital platforms has revolutionized the way in which information is shared and consumed, and this has significant implications for ethnic politics. On one hand, these platforms can be used to spread hate speech and misinformation, fueling ethnic tensions. On the other hand, they can also be used to promote understanding and tolerance, fostering unity among diverse ethnic groups.

In conclusion, it is clear that technology plays a pivotal role in ethnic politics. As we move forward into an increasingly digital age, it is crucial that we continue to explore this relationship and strive to harness the power of technology to promote peace and unity among ethnic groups.

## Chapter: Chapter 20: Ethnicity and the Future
### Introduction
In this chapter, we delve into the intricate relationship between ethnicity and the future. We aim to explore how ethnicity, as a social construct, can shape and influence the political landscape of the future. The dynamics of ethnicity and politics are not static; they evolve with time, influenced by various factors such as demographic changes, socio-economic developments, and technological advancements. 

Ethnicity, as a concept, is deeply rooted in our societies. It is a complex construct that encompasses elements such as shared history, culture, language, and common ancestry. It is a significant factor in shaping individual and collective identities, influencing social interactions, and determining political affiliations. 

In the realm of politics, ethnicity can play a pivotal role. It can be a source of unity, fostering a sense of belonging and shared purpose among members of the same ethnic group. Conversely, it can also be a source of division, leading to conflicts and tensions between different ethnic groups. 

As we look towards the future, it is crucial to understand how these dynamics might evolve. Will ethnicity continue to play a significant role in politics? How might changes in the ethnic composition of societies influence political dynamics? What role will technology play in shaping the relationship between ethnicity and politics? These are some of the questions that we will explore in this chapter.

In the absence of a crystal ball, we cannot predict the future with certainty. However, by examining current trends and drawing on historical patterns, we can make informed projections about the potential impact of ethnicity on future politics. This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive and nuanced understanding of these complex dynamics, paving the way for more informed discussions and policy-making. 

Join us as we embark on this journey of exploration and discovery, delving into the fascinating world of ethnic politics and its potential future trajectories.

### Section: 20.1 Ethnicity and Future Trends:
#### Subsection: 20.1a Ethnicity and Future Demographic Trends
The demographic landscape of the world is continually evolving, and ethnicity plays a significant role in this transformation. As we look towards the future, it is essential to understand how changes in ethnic composition might influence political dynamics. 

In the United Kingdom, for instance, research by academic David Coleman suggests a future demographic decline of the White British population. His projections indicate that the White British could become a minority in Birmingham and London during the 2020s, and by 2056 to 2066, the United Kingdom could have an overall white minority[^1^]. 

This demographic shift is not unique to the United Kingdom. In North America, similar trends are observed. In Canada, scholars Eric Kaufmann and Matthew Goodwin predict that White Canadians will be in the minority by around 2050[^2^]. In the United States, non-Hispanic White Americans under 18 are already a minority as of 2020, and it is projected that non-Hispanic Whites overall will become a minority within the US by 2045[^3^].

These demographic trends have significant implications for the political landscape. As the ethnic composition of societies changes, so too does the political dynamics within these societies. Ethnicity, as a social construct, can influence political affiliations, shape policy preferences, and determine voting behavior. As such, shifts in ethnic demographics can lead to shifts in political power and influence.

However, it is important to note that these demographic trends do not occur in a vacuum. They are influenced by a range of factors, including immigration policies, birth rates, and socio-economic developments. Furthermore, the impact of these demographic shifts on politics is not predetermined. It is shaped by a range of factors, including the political institutions in place, the socio-economic context, and the cultural norms and values of a society.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into these dynamics, exploring how changes in ethnic demographics might influence political dynamics, and how societies and political systems might adapt to these changes.

[^1^]: Coleman, D. (2010). Projections of the ethnic minority populations of the United Kingdom 2006–2056. Population and Development Review, 36(3), 441-486.

[^2^]: Kaufmann, E., & Goodwin, M. (2018). The future of White Canadians: Demographic change and ethnic voting in Canada. Canadian Journal of Political Science, 51(2), 379-405.

[^3^]: Frey, W. H. (2018). The US will become 'minority white' in 2045, Census projects. The Brookings Institution.
